She quickly ducked into the office to drop off the clipboard and samples, then immediately popped back out onto the deck to greet them.

“There she is!” Jack exclaimed happily, quickly approaching his sister and offering her a hug.

He gave his sister a hug, and Clare said to Sarah, "I think your brother had a weird afternoon. He'll tell you about it later.”

Jack’s cheeks flushed slightly. He had already decided that tonight, once they were all home, he would tell them everything—the whole bizarre, creepy story about Delores and the empty dorm room. He needed their collective wisdom and support.

Sarah gave him a questioning, concerned look but just squeezed his arm in support. "Okay. Well, I'm glad you're here now. I just have to run these tests really quick, then I'll be right back out."

She disappeared into the office for a few minutes. Inside, she methodically tested the water samples for chlorine and pH levels. Everything came back within the expected, safe parameters. With her duties complete, she emerged back onto the pool deck, her professional focus shifting to relief and happiness at seeing her family.

"Alright, all good," she announced. She looked at the main lifeguard stand. "It's about time for rotation anyway. I'll go relieve Jen up there. You two have a good swim!"

She gave Jack's arm another squeeze before heading over to the main stand to relieve the other guard.

After a relaxing swim that did wonders to clear his head, it was time to get going. Jack offered both Sarah and Clare one last hug before he and Clare went their separate ways to the locker rooms.

Jack came out to the hallway where Clare was waiting. They made their way to the front desk where Jennifer, his boss, was standing, looking concerned. "Jack? Are you available this evening by chance? We just had a call-out while you were in the pool."

Jack stopped and asked, “What hours did you need?”

“Now until close,” Jennifer said.

The idea of grounding himself in a familiar routine was incredibly appealing after the chaos of the afternoon. He turned to Clare. "Do I have a city shirt in the car by chance?"

“Yes, I think so,” Clare replied.

“We can check,” Jack said to Jennifer. “I have my badge because it also has my rec membership.”

“Oh, we have spare shirts if that’s all you need,” Jennifer replied with a relieved smile.

Clare smiled. “Then let me go get your water bottle and I will let Sarah know that she needs to bring you home.”

Jack did have his city shirt in the car. He clocked in and got settled in for the day, taking care of the scan station. The rec center had about an hour to go before close, and Sarah, having finished her shift, found a seat in the front lobby to do homework, perched where she could keep a discrete eye on Jack, a silent, supportive presence while he worked.

### Part 3: Closing Down a Rec Center

The front lobby of Fairlawn Recreation Center was quiet at 4:51 PM on a Saturday in late January. The last of the families had trickled out, leaving only the sterile scent of chlorine and the low hum of the vending machines. Sarah Bennett sat at a table, her laptop open, using the hour before her brother closed up to get a head start on a college paper. Her table faced the front desk, a habit born of a lifetime of looking out for Jack.

Behind the desk, the closing-time rhythm was underway. Bob, the acting shift supervisor, was quietly counting one of the cash drawers, his register already shut down. Jean, Tyler’s younger sister, had finished with her register as well and was tidying up the counters. Only Tyler’s register was still technically open, though there were no patrons left to serve.

The main doors slid open, and Tyler was the first to look up, his customer service smile ready. A young woman he didn't recognize walked in, her eyes scanning the lobby. Delores’s gaze swept past the empty tables and landed on Tyler, the only open cashier. She gave him a quick, dismissive side-eye, a look that cataloged and filed him away in an instant: *a potential backup, in case this doesn't work out*. Jean, standing nearby, caught the look. Her expression hardened slightly, a flicker of sisterly protectiveness rising in her. She was prepared to step in if this woman gave her brother any trouble.

Then, Delores saw Jack at the scan station, and her focus narrowed. Her target was here. She bypassed the main counter and walked directly to him, her expression shifting from predatory assessment to nervous vulnerability. Sarah, watching from her table, saw Jack’s posture shift instantly. His shoulders, relaxed just a moment before, were now tense.

From behind the desk, Jack felt the air change. When he looked up, a knot of ice formed in his stomach. It was Delores. The dread he’d felt in that dorm room came rushing back. He immediately straightened up, his hands resting on the counter, the shield of professionalism his only defense.

“Can I help you?” he asked, his voice even and cool.

“Hi, Jack,” Delores said softly. “I… I came to apologize. For the other day.”

Sarah leaned back slightly in her chair, her typing forgotten. Across the lobby, Bob paused his counting, and Tyler and Jean exchanged a quick, subtle glance. They could all feel the sudden shift in the atmosphere.

Jack didn’t respond. He just watched her, his mind a battlefield between memory and her seemingly remorseful performance.

“You were right to leave,” Delores continued, her voice gaining a slight tremor. “I was completely out of line. I scared you. I am so, so sorry.” Her eyes looked shiny. “The truth is,” she leaned in slightly, “I just got out of a really... controlling relationship. A guy who... he tried to isolate me, didn't like me having friends...”

Sarah’s eyes narrowed. *A controlling ex? How convenient.* The story sounded like a perfectly crafted script.

Delores focused back on Jack. “When we were talking, you were just so kind, and I haven’t felt that comfortable with someone in a long time. I completely misread everything and got carried away. That’s not who I am.”

Jack felt a flicker of uncomfortable empathy. His gut was screaming that this was a performance, but his heart ached for anyone who had been hurt that way.

“I was just hoping,” Delores’s plea came, soft and desperate, “you’d give me one chance to prove I’m not that person. Just one cup of coffee. Five minutes. If you still think I’m a total creep, I will disappear and never, ever bother you again. Please?”

The word "coffee" landed with an almost audible thud in the quiet lobby. Behind the desk, Bob stopped counting and shared a brief, knowing look with Tyler, who subtly shook his head. Jean had to quickly turn away to hide a smirk. Every single one of them knew about Jack’s well-established preference for hot chocolate. They’d seen him make it in the break room a hundred times. This girl, with her dramatic apology, clearly didn’t know the first thing about him.

That detail solidified it for Sarah. This was all a lie. But she watched her brother, saw the war happening behind his eyes. He was trapped by his own good nature.

He let out a slow breath. “Okay,” he heard himself say, the word tasting like a mistake. “Just coffee.”

A brilliant, relieved smile flashed across Delores’s face, and for a split second, both Jack and Sarah saw it—that triumphant, hollow intensity in her eyes. Then it was gone, replaced by a look of overwhelming gratitude. “Thank you,” she breathed. “You won’t regret it.”

As they exchanged numbers, Delores glanced down at the ID badge clipped to Jack’s belt. Her eyes flickered over his city-issued ID, which clearly read JACK BENNETT, and then to the simple plastic name tag above it that just said JACK. It was the first time she had ever seen his last name. The information registered with a detached coolness, a data point she was finally acquiring, far too late in the game.

Sarah said nothing. She watched Delores leave, her expression hardening from suspicion to a cold, quiet certainty. She knew manipulation when she heard it. She knew her brother. And she knew, with absolute conviction, that this woman was dangerous. She saved her document, closed her laptop, and began to pack her bag.

She is so lucky Jack is who he is, Sarah thought, her anger a cold, hard knot in her stomach. He could have turned to Bob and had her asked to leave the second she started her act. Instead, he’s giving her a chance she never earned, all because he wouldn't know how to hurt someone. She shook her head. Whatever Delores had done at the university to make Jack look like he’d seen a ghost, she was now trying to cover it up with the worst performance Sarah had ever seen. The ride home was going to be a very, very serious conversation.

Behind the front desk, Jean and Tyler shared a similar thought as they watched Delores exit. *She has no idea how good she just got it,* Jean thought, remembering the predatory way Delores had looked at her brother. *Jack’s too nice. Anyone else would have shut that down immediately.*

At precisely 5:00 PM, Bob looked over at Jean. "Go ahead, Jean."

Jean nodded, walked to one of the front desk phones, pressed the 'Page' button, and dialed '00'. A soft beep echoed from the phone, signaling the connection to the building's PA system. She picked up the receiver.

Her clear, professional voice echoed through the lobby and the rest of the facility: "May I have your attention please. The time is now 5:00 PM, and Fairlawn Recreation Center is now closed. Please make your way to the exit at this time. Again, the time is now 5:00 PM and Fairlawn Recreation Center is now closed. Please make your way to the exit at this time."

She hung up the receiver, then immediately pressed the 'Forward All' button on the phone, followed by the 'Voicemail' button. Any incoming calls to the main line would now be sent directly to a recorded message stating the center was closed and providing its operating hours. With the phones now secured for the night, Bob locked the main doors. He looked over at Jack, who was still standing at the scan station, looking pale and lost in thought.

“Hey, Jack,” Bob called out, his voice a calm, steady presence. “Come on back here and give me a hand with the final count, will ya?”

It was a plausible request, but the real reason was a quiet exercise of his duty of care as the shift supervisor. Bob had seen the whole strange encounter and wanted to make sure his employee was okay.

As Jack walked behind the counter, Bob turned to the two youngest members of his crew. “You two alright? That was… a little weird.”

Tyler just shrugged, but his younger sister Jean spoke up, her voice low but fierce. “I’m fine,” she said, glancing at her brother. “But I saw the way that girl looked at him when she came in. I saw right through her from the beginning. She was trouble.” Her protective stance was clear.

Bob nodded, appreciating her candor. He then focused on Jack, and together they finished the final cash counts and closing procedures in a comfortable, professional silence.

At 5:08 PM, everything was balanced and locked up. “Alright, that’s us,” Bob said, initialing the final report. “Go on home, Jack. Get some rest.” As far as the time clock was concerned, 5:08 was close enough to Jack's scheduled 5:15 PM departure to not matter.

Jack clocked out, grateful for the familiar, grounding routine and for his supervisor’s unspoken support. He grabbed his things and met Sarah by the door. At the same time, Tyler and Jean finished their own closing duties and headed for the exit as well.

As they walked out into the cold January night, Sarah, still feeling unsettled and protective after the encounter with Delores, put her arm around Jack’s shoulders, pulling him into a comforting side-hug. Jack immediately responded in kind, wrapping his own arm around her waist, leaning into her familiar, safe presence as they walked toward their car.

Jean, walking a few feet behind them with her brother, saw the easy, physical affection between the siblings. A small giggle escaped her lips. She imagined what that strange, intense woman, Delores, would think if she saw them now. She’d probably assume the worst, completely incapable of understanding what Jean saw so clearly: two very close siblings who loved each other and were not afraid to show it with simple, non-sexual affection. Feeling her own surge of sisterly protectiveness after witnessing the strange encounter, Jean moved a little closer to Tyler as they walked, a small, silent gesture of solidarity and protection for her own brother.

### Part 4: Conversations on a Ride Home

The silence in the car was a shared, weary comfort as Jack pulled out of the Fairlawn Recreation Center parking lot. He and Sarah were both tired, the end of a long week and the weight of the last few months pressing on them. Jack was driving, his hands steady on the wheel.

A shrill, tinny ringtone echoed from a basic flip phone resting in the center console. Jack glanced at the screen, saw it was his cousins, and tapped the button to connect it to the car’s speaker system.

“Hey! Are you guys on the road yet?” Clare’s voice came through the speakers, clear and bright.

“Just pulled out,” Jack said. “You’re on speaker.”

“Perfect!” Molly’s voice joined in. “We were just staring at this mountain of boxes and figured we needed a distraction. Have you guys started packing the kitchen yet?”

Sarah sighed, a smile touching her lips. “Don’t even say the word ‘packing.’ I think if I see another roll of tape, I’ll scream. But no, we’re saving the kitchen for last. Dad’s orders.”

“Smart man,” Clare said. “Uncle Devin is the same way. He says the coffee maker is the last thing to go in a box and the first thing to come out.”

They chatted for a few minutes about the upcoming move on February 17th. The logistics were daunting, but the thought of their two families finally being under one roof after the raw, recent pain of the divorce was a beacon for all of them. The anger at their mother’s betrayal was still fresh, making them cling to this new, redefined family unit even more fiercely.

“So, how was the rest of the shift?” Molly asked Jack. “Anything exciting happen in the last nine minutes of a Saturday at the rec?”

Jack hesitated, glancing at Sarah. “Actually, yeah. That girl from the VDU open house showed up.”

Clare’s voice sharpened with immediate recognition over the speaker. “Wait, the ‘intense’ one? The one you told me about? What did she want?”

Sarah took a deep breath. As the sole witness, she knew it was her turn. She recounted the entire scene—Delores’s hesitant entrance, her carefully worded apology, the perfectly crafted sob story about a controlling ex.

Over the speaker, Clare scoffed. “A controlling ex? That sounds way too convenient. That’s exactly the kind of manipulative vibe you were telling me about, Jack.”

“It was a total performance,” Sarah confirmed, her voice low. “And then she asked him for coffee.”

“Coffee?” Molly chimed in, incredulous. “She asked *Jack* for coffee? Jack, you hate coffee!”

“That’s what I told him!” Sarah said, relieved to be validated.

Faced with this unified front of concern, Jack felt his own unease solidify into certainty. He wasn’t defensive; he was just tired.

“I know,” he said, his voice quiet. “My gut was screaming the whole time. But… she was standing there, telling me this story about being hurt… I wouldn't even know how to be cruel enough to just shut her down.”

“We know, Jack,” Clare said gently through the speaker. “That’s what makes you, you. But it’s also what people like her count on.”

“So what did you do?” Molly asked.

“I agreed to get coffee,” Jack admitted. “Just once. To hear her out and then end it.”

There was a brief silence on the line. Then, Sarah spoke, her voice full of understanding. “Okay. That makes sense. Just… be careful, Jack. Please. The last thing any of us need right now is more drama from some random girl, not after everything with Mom.”

“Agreed,” the twins on the phone said in unison.

“I will,” Jack promised, and he meant it. He felt a profound sense of gratitude for them. They were his compass, his anchor.

“Okay, enough about that weirdo,” Clare announced, her tone shifting back to business. “Back to the move. Who gets the bedroom with the bigger closet in the new house, because Molly is already trying to claim it…”

The conversation shifted back to logistics, the impending danger of Delores acknowledged, assessed, and contained by their collective vigilance. Jack drove on, listening to their familiar, loving arguments, feeling profoundly safe in their circle of protection.

### Part 5: A Safe Harbor

Later that evening, after the family had finished one of their last dinners at Jermaine's house, the men—Jermaine, Devin, and Sam—settled in the living room to watch a basketball game. The low murmur of the television provided the perfect cover. Jack, his expression a mask of anxiety, caught Sarah’s eye and gave a slight nod towards his bedroom. She understood instantly.

Molly and Clare, who had been waiting for this conversation since the phone call in the car, followed them without a word. The four of them gathered in Jack’s room, the door closed, creating a private, safe space away from the rest of the family. Jack was too scared and embarrassed to tell his dad, his uncle, or his grandfather the full, ugly details of his afternoon. But here, with his three women, he knew he could say anything.

He sat on the edge of his bed, and they gathered around him, their presence a silent, supportive circle.

“Okay, Jack,” Sarah said gently. “We’re all here. What really happened?”

He took a shaky breath and began to tell them everything. He told them about the private tour, the dark hallway, and the long lunch where Delores had so expertly lowered his guard. And then he got to the hardest part, his voice dropping, his gaze fixed on the floor.

“She took me to this empty apartment-style dorm,” he stammered. “And she… she led me straight to the bedroom. She said we could… you know… do things there.” He couldn’t bring himself to say the word sex. “I think… I think she was going to let me have a highly inappropriate encounter with her.”

He recounted the kiss, the disorienting speed of it all, and the final, horrifying moment of clarity. “I looked in her eyes,” he whispered, “and there was nothing there. It was like she was looking through me. Like I wasn’t even a person. I was just… a thing she could use.”

Sarah, Molly, and Clare exchanged horrified, angry glances over his head.

“So I stopped it,” Jack finished, his voice trembling slightly. “I told her I had to go and I left.”

“Jack, that’s not just ‘intense’,” Sarah said, her voice tight with a protective fury. “That’s predatory.”

“You absolutely did the right thing by leaving,” Clare added, her own voice shaking with anger on his behalf.

He finally looked up at them, his eyes full of confusion and self-doubt. “I know. But then she showed up at the rec center, with that story about her ex, and she was crying… and I just… I couldn’t be a jerk,” he explained, the words tumbling out. “It’s my character flaw. I wouldn’t know how to hurt someone, how to just tell her to go away, even when my gut was screaming at me.”

“That’s not a flaw, Jack,” Molly said softly, putting a hand on his knee. “That’s your kindness. And she used it against you.”

He had told them everything, and they didn’t judge him. They understood. Their faces weren’t filled with disappointment, but with a fierce, unified resolve. They knew the danger was real, and they knew it was up to them to be the wall he needed.

Seeing his vulnerability and the shame warring with his relief, Sarah knew what he needed most. “Oh, Jack. Come here.”

She moved to sit beside him on the bed, and Molly and Clare closed in, their own arms wrapping around him and Sarah. They enveloped him in a gentle, protective group hug, a circle of unwavering, unconditional love. They didn’t care that he had been manipulated or that he felt flawed; they just cared that he was safe. In that moment, surrounded by the three most important women in his life, Jack finally let the last of his tension go. He was loved, no matter what.

### Part 6: Coffee Date

The coffee shop in the Virginia Dominion University library was quiet for a Saturday afternoon. Jack pushed the door open, the scent of roasted coffee beans and old books filling the air. He felt a familiar knot of dread in his stomach. The conversation he’d had with Sarah and his cousins in the car echoed in his mind: *It sounded like a script... She doesn’t know the first thing about you.* He was here to end this, not to start it.

He saw Delores at a small table in the corner. She looked up and gave him a small, hopeful smile that didn’t quite reach her eyes. He walked to the counter, his hands shoved in his pockets.

“Hey,” she said, joining him in the short line. “Thanks for coming. My treat, I insist.”

“I can get my own,” Jack said politely but firmly.

At the register, Delores ordered a black coffee. The barista turned to Jack. “Can I get for you?”

“A hot chocolate, please,” Jack said. “With milk, and whipped cream and chocolate syrup on top.”

He pulled out his wallet to pay. Delores started to protest, her hand moving forward. “No, really, I—” she began, her tone insistent, but she caught herself, quickly masking the controlling impulse. She forced a small, apologetic laugh. “Sorry. Habit. My ex always made a big deal about who paid. You just go ahead.”

Jack noticed the brief flash of annoyance in her eyes before she covered it. It was a tiny crack in her performance, but it was there. It was the same hollow intensity he’d seen in the dorm. He paid for his decadent drink, the transaction feeling like a small but necessary victory for his own autonomy.

They sat down, and an awkward silence stretched between them. Delores was the one to break it, leaning forward earnestly.

“Look, Jack,” she began, “I just want to apologize again. For how I came on so strong. I told you I just got out of a really controlling relationship,” she said, launching into the projected version of her history with Leo. “He tried to isolate me, made me feel like I was always walking on eggshells. I guess I just forgot how to act around someone who is actually a good, gentle person.”

Jack listened, his guard fully up. He heard the words, but all he could think was, *Sarah was right. This is a performance.* The story pricked at his empathy, but the memory of her irritation at the counter was a fresh warning. His inability to be cruel was warring with every self-preservation instinct he had.

“I know I messed up our first impression,” Delores said, her eyes pleading. “But I would really like to get to know you, Jack. For real this time. No pressure. Please, just give me a second chance. One real date.”

He looked at her, at the practiced vulnerability in her expression. His gut was screaming no. His sister and cousins’ worried voices were a chorus in his head. But the thought of looking this person in the eye and saying, “No, I don’t believe you, go away,” felt impossibly cruel. He wouldn’t even know how to form the words.

A long, tense moment passed. He stared into his mug of whipped cream and chocolate, feeling trapped by his own compassion. He let out a slow, quiet sigh of resignation.

“Okay,” he said, the word feeling heavy and wrong. “One date.”

The relief that washed over Delores’s face was immediate, and again, for just a second, he saw that flash of triumph, of victory. She quickly hid it behind a grateful smile.

“Thank you,” she said, her voice soft. “You won’t regret it.”

But as Jack sat there, finishing his hot chocolate while she talked about a class she was taking, he knew he already did.

## Chapter 6: A Date with Delores

### Part 1: An Unassuming Dinner

The drive to Delores’s apartment was quiet, with Jack and his cousin Molly sharing the front seat of her car. As Molly pulled into a parking spot, she glanced at Jack. His face was a mask of polite neutrality, but she could see the tension in his shoulders. “You sure about this?” she asked softly.

Jack just shrugged, his eyes not meeting hers. “It’s just dinner. I told her I would.”

As they got out and walked towards the apartment building’s main door, Molly instinctively started to loop her arm through Jack’s, a familiar gesture of comfort and closeness between them. But she stopped herself, letting her hand drop to her side. Jack’s unease about this girl was palpable, and Molly needed to be an impartial set of eyes, taking mental notes without the distraction of their usual closeness. Her guard was already up on his behalf.

When they arrived, Delores met them at the door, her smile a little too bright. Inside, the apartment was meticulously clean but felt impersonal. An older woman with Delores’s intense eyes emerged from the kitchen.

“You must be Jack,” she said. “I’m Robyn, Delores’s mother. And you are?”

“My cousin, Molly,” Jack said. “She drove me.”

“Well, come in, both of you!” Robyn said with a practiced politeness that felt brittle. Molly was the only one who noticed it—a subtle, sharp tension emanating from Robyn that was directed not at her guests, but at her own daughter. She kept the observation to herself, another mental note in a growing list.

“You’re just in time. Delores, set another place for Molly. You have to stay and try my world-famous lasagna.”

“Oh, you don’t have to—” Molly started to protest.

“Nonsense!” Delores insisted. “Mom’s lasagna is legendary. You have to stay.”

Molly, not wanting to make a scene, reluctantly agreed. They ate in the small dining area. As Robyn served the main course, Molly noticed the foil tray it came from—a family-sized portion from the freezer aisle at ValueMart. She kept a neutral expression, but made a mental note. *Mental Note \#1: "World-famous" lasagna comes from the freezer aisle.* Jack, oblivious, simply accepted the food.

### Part 2: Dinner with Robyn and Delores

Robyn returned from the kitchen bearing a steaming casserole dish, the scent of tomato and cheese filling the small dining area. As she served generous, perfectly rectangular portions onto their plates, Molly confirmed her suspicion. The layers were a little too uniform, the sauce a little too generic. It was definitely a high-quality frozen lasagna from ValueMart, heated and served as homemade. She took a bite, offering a polite smile. *Mental Note \#2: They lie about small, pointless things.*

“It’s very good, Robyn. Thank you,” Molly said, her voice neutral.

“Isn’t it?” Delores chimed in, beaming at Jack. “My mom’s lasagna is the best. She’s been making this recipe for years.”

“Well, it’s nice to have someone in this house who appreciates a home-cooked meal,” Robyn said, her voice sweet but with a sharp edge. Her gaze flicked toward Delores for a fraction of a second before settling back on Jack. The comment hung in the air, a clear but unspoken accusation.

Molly’s eyes narrowed slightly. *Mental Note \#3: There’s serious tension between them. That wasn't a joke.* Jack, oblivious to the undercurrent, simply took another bite of lasagna.

Robyn then turned her full, intense focus onto Jack. “And Jack, Delores tells me you work at Fairlawn. It’s good you have a steady job. Responsibility is so important in a young man. Some people just don't seem to understand that making foolish choices has consequences that can follow you for a very long time.” Again, her eyes flickered to Delores, a silent judgment passing between mother and daughter.

Molly saw it clearly this time. The subtext was thick with a history Molly didn't know, but its weight was palpable. Robyn wasn't just talking about a job; she was making a pointed, veiled reference to some significant, costly mistake from Delores's recent past. The barb was personal, and it was sharp. *Mental Note \#4: The mother is deeply angry at Delores about something that sounds like it cost a lot of money, and she's using Jack to punish her for it.*

Jack, caught in the middle, just felt a prickle of discomfort, unsure why the compliments felt so loaded. “It’s a good job,” he said simply.

Delores, ignoring the silent jab, doubled down on her praise for Jack, her laughter a little too loud. “He’s so responsible. Not like some people.”

The passive-aggressive volley was dizzying. The conversation continued in this vein—superficially pleasant but lacking any real depth. Delores talked about a professor she liked at VDU. Robyn asked Jack about the types of programs the rec center offered. Jack and Molly gave polite, brief answers, volunteering little. Every topic felt like a carefully managed performance by Delores and her mother, designed to present a picture of perfect, unthreatening normalcy. Molly continued to observe, her initial unease solidifying with every rehearsed question and overly bright smile. This wasn’t a family dinner; it was an audition, and she was watching a silent, ugly war play out right in front of her.

### Part 3: The Interrogation

The dinner conversation remained superficially pleasant, but Molly felt like she was watching a play. Every word from Delores and Robyn felt rehearsed, every question designed to gather information.

“We were thinking of going to that new sci-fi movie next weekend,” Molly said, turning to Jack with a familiar, easy smile. “All four of us, if you’re not working.”

“Oh, that’s so sweet that you’re all still so close,” Delores interjected, her voice dripping with artificial sugariness. As she spoke, she placed her hand on Jack’s forearm, a gesture that looked like affection but felt like an anchor. “It’s important for a man to have his own life, though. Not to just be tied to his family all the time.”

The jab was subtle but clear. Jack tensed at her touch, a flash of annoyance cutting through his polite demeanor. Molly’s smile tightened. *Mental Note \#5: She’s possessive and actively belittles his family bond.*

After the meal, Molly stood up. “Thank you for dinner, Robyn. It was lovely. But I should get going.” She turned to Jack. “Text me whenever you’re ready to be picked up, okay?”

“Will do,” Jack said, standing to walk her to the door.

In the entryway, Molly gave her cousin a firm, comforting hug. “Call if you need anything,” she whispered. “Anything at all.” Over Jack’s shoulder, she saw Delores watching them, her polite smile frozen, but her eyes were cold and hard.

As Molly walked to her car, her instincts screamed. The passive-aggressive digs from Robyn, Delores's possessive comment about their family, the frozen stare during their goodbye hug—it was all wrong. *She’s not leaving him here,* Molly decided. She drove down the block, parked where she had a clear view of the apartment building’s entrance, and settled in to wait.

Back inside, Jack returned to the living room to find Robyn staring at him. Her expression had shifted. Her eyes softened in a way that was deeply strange, her lips parting in a small, familiar smile that felt entirely inappropriate. She was looking at him, but as if she were picturing his father. It made his skin crawl.

“I know your father, Jermaine, from work, you know,” she said, her voice taking on a cooing quality. “Same employer, different offices. I heard through the grapevine about his difficult divorce.” She leaned forward, her gaze unwavering and unnervingly intimate. Robyn was still seething over the trouble Delores had brought to their door—the screaming phone calls from her sister, the humiliation, and most of all, the massive, unpayable debt Delores now owed to that Porter family after the disastrous trip to Washington state. But looking at Jack, a new, opportunistic thought took root. A single man, a good job… maybe *he* was the solution. Or rather, his father was. If Jermaine was lonely, maybe he’d be grateful for some company, grateful enough to help with a little financial trouble… a meal ticket.

“How is he *really* doing?” she pressed. “It must be so hard on him, being all alone now.”

“He’s getting by,” Jack said, his answer clipped. He felt a desperate need to protect his father’s privacy from this strange, invasive questioning.

“You have your father’s eyes,” Robyn continued, her voice a low purr. “He always has such a kind, handsome look about him at those company functions. A man like that… he shouldn’t be alone for long. Has he… started to get out at all? Seeing people?”

The questions were too personal, too invasive. And the way she was looking at him, with that strange, lovesick expression, was profoundly creepy. Delores watched them from the sofa, a small, knowing smirk on her face, enjoying his discomfort. This wasn’t a date; it was an interrogation with a bizarre, unsettling agenda. He felt the overwhelming urge to leave.

### Part 4: The Manipulation

The questions were too personal, too invasive. The way Robyn was looking at him with that strange, lovesick expression was profoundly creepy. And Delores, watching from the sofa with a small, knowing smirk, was enjoying his discomfort. It was in that moment that another detail from earlier clicked into place for Jack: the cold, hard look in Delores’s eyes when he had hugged Molly goodbye. It hadn't just been a fleeting expression; it was a flash of raw, possessive anger. *Mental Note: She hates it when I hug my cousins.*

That was it. He was done. The creepy interrogation from the mother and the memory of his girlfriend's silent rage were the final red flags he needed.

“Wow, look at the time,” Jack said, forcing a casual tone as he stood up. “I should probably get going.”

“Oh, don’t be silly,” Delores said, moving from her chair to block his path to the door. Her hand rested on his arm. Internally, she was seething. *The way that girl hugged him, so familiar. Like she owned him. He’s mine.* But her voice was a soft purr. “Mom’s just being nosy. Don’t mind her. The night's still young.”

Jack felt a prickle of panic. He gently removed her hand. “No, really, I should go.”

“Please, Jack?” Delores said, her voice dropping, her expression shifting to one of profound hurt. “Don’t let my weird mom scare you off. I’m so sorry about that. I promise, I’ll never go too fast for you. I’ll go at whatever pace you’re comfortable with.”

Her promise felt wrong, like another line from a script. His gut told him to run. He pulled out his phone, turning away from her slightly, and sent a quick text to Molly: Ready now.

Delores saw the motion, and her eyes narrowed. “Are you texting your cousin to come get you? Already?” Her voice was no longer soft; it was wounded and accusatory.

Caught, Jack stammered, “Uh, yeah, she’s just waiting…”

“Wow,” Delores said, taking a step back and wrapping her arms around herself as if she were cold. “Okay. I get it. My mom was weird, and I was weird, and now you’re bailing. It’s… it’s actually exactly what my ex used to do.”

The guilt trip was so sudden and sharp it felt like a physical blow. Jack froze.

“Anytime things got a little complicated or weren't perfect,” she continued, her voice now thick with unshed tears, “he’d just shut down and run. He couldn’t handle it. I really, truly thought you were different, Jack. Kinder.”

The accusation hit him precisely where she intended. He hated the idea of being like some cruel ex. His entire being recoiled from the thought of causing someone pain. His own instincts were screaming that *he* was the one in danger, but her words twisted his self-protective impulse into an act of cruelty.

Just then, the apartment’s buzzer rang, a sharp, insistent sound. Molly was downstairs.

Delores flinched at the sound, a single tear tracing a path down her cheek. “Please,” she whispered, her voice breaking. “I know tonight was a disaster. My mom ruined it. Just… one more chance. Not here. A walk in the park. In the daytime. Just to prove to you I’m not… this. Please don’t let me think all guys are the same.”

The buzzer rang again. Molly was waiting. Delores was crying. Jack felt a dizzying wave of guilt and confusion. The request was so small. A walk. To refuse now would be a deliberate, final act of hurting her. He couldn't do it. It wasn't in him.

“Okay,” he breathed, the word feeling like a surrender. “One walk. Next week.”

He fled the apartment, Delores’s whispered “Thank you” following him down the hall. He felt her eyes on him the whole way, a mixture of victory and possession that he was too flustered to fully register.

### Part 5: The Escape and the Hook

The buzzer rang, a sharp, insistent sound that cut through the thick, manipulative atmosphere of the apartment. Molly was here. A wave of immense, profound relief washed over Jack, so potent it almost made him dizzy.

Delores flinched at the sound, her performance kicking into high gear as a single, perfect crocodile tear traced a path down her cheek. "Please," she whispered, her voice breaking. "I know tonight was a disaster. My mom ruined it. Just… one more chance. Not here. A walk in the park. In the daytime. Just to prove to you I’m not… this. Please don’t let me think all guys are the same."

The buzzer rang again, a welcome, insistent call from the real world. Molly was waiting. Delores was crying. Internally, Delores was seething with a white-hot fury. Her plan was in ruins. She had wanted him in her bed, vulnerable and obligated. She had wanted a repeat of the dorm room, but with a different outcome. She had failed. This pathetic, tearful plea was her last-ditch effort to keep him on the hook.

Jack, trapped between his own kindness and the waiting headlights of his escape, felt his resolve crumble. The request was so small. A walk. To refuse now would be a deliberate, final act of hurting her. He couldn't do it.

“We’ll talk next week, okay?” he heard himself say, the words a concession he immediately regretted.

The change was instantaneous. The moment the words left his mouth, the tears in her eyes simply vanished. The wellspring of her sadness dried up the second she got what she wanted. She gave a single, delicate sniffle for effect, but the performance was over.

“Okay,” she whispered, her voice suddenly steady. She leaned in, and before he could react, she gave him a quick, chaste, and completely calculated kiss on the cheek. She pulled back and opened the door for him. “Drive safe.”

She closed the door before he could respond, leaving him on the stoop, confused and unsettled. The abrupt shift from heartbroken tears to calm composure didn't fully register in his flustered mind, but it added to the deep, gut-level feeling that everything he had just witnessed was a lie.

He practically ran to Molly’s waiting car, pulling the door open and collapsing into the passenger seat. He was so glad she had waited, so happy to see her familiar, safe face. He pulled her into a fierce hug, a gesture of pure relief.

“Whoa,” Molly said, hugging him back tightly. “You okay? I had a feeling you might want to leave sooner rather than later.”

“You have no idea,” Jack breathed, finally feeling like he could breathe again. “Let’s just go home.”

### Part 6: The Debrief

In the quiet cocoon of Molly’s car, the tension from the apartment finally began to dissipate, replaced by a shared, grim understanding. Molly waited until they were a few blocks away, the building's entrance no longer visible in the rearview mirror, before she spoke.

“So,” she said, her voice low. “That was… something.”

Jack let out a long, shuddering breath he didn’t realize he’d been holding. “Something is one word for it. Her mom is really weird, Molly. The way she was asking about Dad… the look on her face… it was so creepy.”

“I saw it,” Molly said, her knuckles white on the steering wheel. “The whole thing felt like a performance. Let’s call the others. They need to hear this.”

Molly gestured to the flip phone in the center console. Jack picked it up, held down the side button, and waited for the beep. “Call Sarah,” he said. The phone chirped and began dialing, the call immediately coming through the car’s speaker system.

“Hey, you guys on your way?” Sarah’s voice came through first. “We’re on speaker,” Clare added.

“Yeah, we’re heading home now,” Molly said. “And listen, the date was a total disaster. I was taking mental notes the whole time.”

“Mental notes?” Clare asked, intrigued.

“Yeah,” Molly said, her voice grim. “Note one: the ‘world-famous’ lasagna was from the ValueMart freezer aisle. They lie about small, pointless things. Note two: Robyn, the mom, is still furious with Delores over something big, and she kept taking passive-aggressive shots at her all through dinner. Note three: Robyn is using Jack to punish Delores. She kept praising him for being responsible and then giving Delores a look like, ‘Why can’t you be like him?’ Note four: Robyn completely controls the conversation and has zero interest in anything Delores has to say.”

“Wow,” Sarah breathed over the speaker. “What a mess.”

“It gets worse,” Jack said, his voice quiet. “I have a mental note of my own. When Molly hugged me goodbye, Delores had this look on her face… like she was furious. Like she hated that Molly was even touching me.”

“I saw that too!” Molly confirmed. “Her eyes were like ice. Which brings me to Mental Note number five: Delores is possessive and she actively belittles your family. She made some snide comment about you needing your own life and not just being tied to us.”

A cold silence fell on the speakerphone.

“Okay, that’s it. You’re done with her,” Sarah said, her voice tight with a protective fury.

“I know,” Jack said, his voice hardening with resolve. “When I end it, I’m going to need backup. I’m going to need your help, all three of you, to tell her it’s over. She’ll just start crying or twist it around like she did tonight. I need her to see a wall, so she knows there’s no way back in.”

“Jack, you just say the word,” Clare said, her voice fierce. “We will be your wall.”

“Always,” Sarah and Molly said in unison.

The conversation shifted to Jack’s request for comfort that night and Sarah’s immediate, loving agreement to stay with him. He felt the tension he’d been holding since walking into that apartment finally begin to release. He had told them everything, and now, he had a plan, and an army at his back.

Jack stared out the passenger window as Molly drove, the streetlights blurring past. The quiet cocoon of her car was a sanctuary, but the creepy feeling from the apartment still clung to him like a film.

“Hey, Mol?” he said, his voice quiet. “Tonight was… really bad. It’s one of those nights. I… I’m going to need someone with me tonight. I know we don't do it all the time, but..."

Molly’s expression softened with immediate understanding. This wasn't a routine request; this was a signal of deep distress. “Of course, Jack,” she said gently. “Don’t even worry about it. Here.” She gestured to the flip phone in the center console. “Call her.”

Jack picked up the phone, held down the side button, and waited for the beep. “Call Sarah,” he said, his voice steady despite the tremor he felt inside. The phone chirped and began dialing, the call immediately coming through the car’s speaker system.

“Hey, you guys on your way?” Sarah’s voice came through. “We’re on speaker,” Clare added, her voice just as clear.

“Yeah, we’re heading home now,” Molly said. “Listen, you two, the date was a total disaster. I was taking mental notes the whole time.”

For the next ten minutes, they recounted the entire evening for Sarah and Clare—the freezer-aisle lasagna, Delores’s possessive comments, and the deeply unsettling interrogation from Robyn about their father.

“I knew it,” Sarah said, her voice tight with anger. “I knew she was trouble. I’m so sorry you had to go through that, Jack.”

“He’s pretty creeped out,” Molly confirmed. “It’s one of those nights, Sarah. He’s going to need you.”

There was no hesitation on the other end of the line. Sarah’s response was immediate and warm. “Understood. Poor guy. Don’t worry about a thing. I'll get his full-sized bed ready for us. I’ve got him.”

A wave of profound relief washed over Jack. He wasn't just being granted a favor; his need for safety was being met without question. A quiet, sobering thought settled in his mind: after tonight, he didn’t think he could face sleeping alone again for a long, long time. This was no longer going to be an occasional thing; from now on, he knew he would always need one of his three favorite women by his side.

“Thanks, Sarah,” he said, his voice thick with emotion.

“Always, Jack,” her voice came back, a warm promise through the car’s speakers. “See you in a few.”

### Part 7: A Theory is Formed

They retreated to the privacy of Jack’s bedroom, the four of them creating a familiar, safe circle. Jack recounted the final, manipulative moments at the apartment door, and the girls listened, their expressions growing grimmer with every word.

“Okay,” Sarah said, pacing the floor after he finished. “So, what is her deal?”

“Well,” Clare chimed in, looking thoughtful, “maybe she *did* have a really bad breakup, and she’s just so messed up from it that she’s become super possessive herself. It’s sad, but it also makes her dangerous for you to be around, Jack. She clearly needs therapy, not a new boyfriend.”

“I don’t think she’s just messed up,” Sarah countered, her voice sharp with a protective fury honed by what she had witnessed at the rec center. “I think she’s a user. She saw you, Jack, saw how kind and maybe a little overwhelmed you were, and decided you were an easy target. That whole story about the ‘controlling ex’ is a line, a script she uses to make her targets feel sorry for her. She doesn't want a boyfriend; she wants someone she can control. She’s mad at us because she knows a strong family is the one thing that can see through her act.”

This theory felt chillingly accurate, a close second place to the real truth. It explained Delores's behavior perfectly based on what Sarah had seen. But Molly, who had been quiet, processing all her mental notes from the dinner, had one more piece to add.

“Okay, this is going to sound crazy,” she began, “but what if you’re both right? What if she’s a user, like you said, Sarah… but what if her story about the controlling ex is true, she just got the roles mixed up?”

The other three looked at her, confused.

“Think about it,” Molly continued, the theory taking shape. “What if *she* was the one who was super controlling? What if she was the abuser, and this guy—whoever he was—finally managed to get away from her?”

A stunned silence fell over the room as the pieces clicked into place.

“And now,” Molly finished, her voice barely a whisper, “she’s freaking out because she lost control. So she’s trying to find someone new—you, Jack—to be a replacement. She’s trying to do it all over again, only this time she thinks she can get it ‘right.’ It would explain everything—why she’s so intense, why she lies about everything, and why her mom is so angry. The last one ended in a huge disaster, and she doesn't want a repeat.”

Jack stared at his cousin, a chill running down his spine. It was a horrifying theory, but it made perfect, terrible sense. It explained the hollow, hungry look in Delores’s eyes. It explained the way her mother used him as a weapon. He wasn’t a person to them; he was a project. A do-over. The thought was sickening.

### Part 8: The New Normal Begins

The weight of the day's events, from the unsettling encounter with Delores to Jack's emotional confession, settled over the four of them in the quiet of his bedroom. The horrifying truth of Molly's theory hung in the air, solidifying their collective resolve.

"Well," Molly said, finally breaking the silence as she stood up, "it's getting late, and we should probably head home."

"Are you going to be okay?" Clare asked Jack, her expression full of concern.

"I will be now," Jack said, and he meant it. He felt seen, understood, and protected.

Molly came to the side of the bed and leaned down, giving him a gentle but firm hug. "You call us for anything," she whispered. "We're your wall, remember?"

Clare hugged him next, just as fiercely. "We love you, Jack."

"Love you guys too," he murmured. "Thanks for... everything."

Sarah walked them to the door, and the sisters exchanged quiet, knowing looks of support before Molly and Clare headed home. When Sarah returned, the room felt even more like a sanctuary.

"Ready to call it a night?" she asked softly.

Jack just nodded, the exhaustion of the day finally catching up to him. They went through the simple, quiet motions of getting ready for bed. Sarah made sure he had his water on the nightstand and that his pillows were comfortable. She changed into her own pajamas, the easy, matter-of-fact lack of privacy a new and comforting normal for them.

She slid into the queen bed beside him. From this night forward, this would be their routine. One of his three trusted females would always be there, a steady presence in the dark. Tonight, it was Sarah.

He rolled onto his side, and she immediately moved in behind him, spooning him in a protective, comforting embrace. She felt the tension in his shoulders begin to melt away as he leaned back into her.

"Try to get some sleep," she whispered into his hair.

"Okay," he murmured, his eyes already drifting shut. For the first time since he'd walked into that dorm room at VDU, he felt completely and utterly safe.

## Chapter 8: Planning Sarah’s Birthday

### Part 1: A Mission for the Sister

A week or so after the disastrous dinner date, on a Saturday afternoon, Jack, Molly, and Clare made a special trip to the Dominion Tower Mall in Norfolk. The official mission: find a perfect 23rd birthday present for Sarah. Her 23rd was a big deal, not just because it was her birthday, but because she had also just been officially promoted to Head Lifeguard at Fairlawn, and they wanted to get her something that celebrated both milestones. They had chosen this specific mall because it had a specialty outdoor gear store that wasn't in any of the malls closer to their Virginia Beach home, and they were on the hunt for a specific high-end pair of polarized sunglasses Sarah had been wanting.

They walked through the bustling mall concourse as a single, cohesive unit. Jack was in the middle, his arms draped comfortably over his cousins' shoulders. Molly and Clare, in turn, each had an arm wrapped around his waist, their linked arms and easy strides a testament to their deep, platonic affection. They were a small island of familial comfort in the sea of weekend shoppers.

"Okay, so what are we thinking?" Clare asked, her voice bright with the energy of the mission. "She's been dropping hints about needing a new, really good pair of sunglasses for when she's on the main stand."

"That's a good idea," Molly added, her analytical mind already working. "But we should also get her something fun. Something just for her, not for work. She loves that author who writes those cozy mystery novels."

"Both are great ideas," Jack said, feeling more relaxed and himself than he had in weeks, safely buffered by his two favorite cousins. "We can hit the sunglass store first, then the bookstore."

Their shopping trip was a success. At the outdoor gear store, they found the exact stylish but durable pair of polarized sunglasses they knew Sarah wanted. At the bookstore a little while later, they found the latest novel by her favorite mystery author. With their mission accomplished and their stomachs starting to rumble, a new consensus was quickly reached.

"I'm starving," Jack announced. "Food court?"

"Definitely," Molly and Clare said in unison. "It's on the third floor, right?"

"Yep," Jack confirmed. "Let's go."

They made their way towards the escalators, still walking in their familiar, linked formation.

### Part 2: A Taste of Twin Magic

“Okay,” Molly said, “it’s going to be impossible for all three of us to navigate that line and find a table. You two go scout for a place to sit. I’ll be the food warrior and get our order.”

“Sounds like a plan,” Jack said. “Usual for everyone?”

“You know it,” Molly confirmed with a smile before heading into the fray at the popular burger counter.

Jack and Clare, meanwhile, began to navigate the crowded seating area. Jack put his arm around his cousin’s shoulders, leaning on her for a bit of support in the noisy crowd. Clare responded in kind, wrapping her arm around his waist.

They were just about to claim a newly vacated table when a voice, dripping with false sweetness, cut through the food court chatter.

“Well, hello, Molly.”

Jack and Clare froze. It was Delores. She stood directly in front of them, her eyes fixed on Clare with a look of pure disdain.

*Unbelievable,* Delores thought, a surge of resentment rising in her. *Still attached at his hip, I see.*

The young woman with Jack looked at Delores, her expression one of polite, genuine confusion. Clare knew exactly who this was from Jack's stories and the family debriefs, but this was her first time laying eyes on the infamous Delores. A spark of mischief and protective anger lit within her; she decided to play dumb. “I’m sorry, have we met?” Clare asked, her tone open and friendly.

Delores’s smile faltered. She looked from Clare's face to Jack's. This was a trick. It had to be. "Very funny," she scoffed. "You know we met. At my apartment. You're Jack's cousin, Molly."

Clare just tilted her head, her expression still one of complete, innocent bewilderment. She was enjoying this immensely. “Molly?” she repeated, her brow furrowed in feigned thought. “I’m sorry, I don’t think I know a Molly. Is she a friend of yours?”

“Stop it,” Delores hissed, her frustration growing. “You look exactly like her.”

“I do?” Clare said, her eyes wide with feigned surprise. “Wow, that’s so weird! I’ll have to tell her you said that, if I ever meet her. Small world!”

Delores stared, her mind unable to process the situation. Was this girl really this dumb? Or was she an incredible actor? Her manipulative plan was dissolving into a puddle of pure, flustered confusion. She was about to say something truly nasty when a new voice came from behind her.

"Hey guys, sorry that took forever. The line was insane."

Delores froze. She turned around slowly. Standing there, holding a tray laden with burgers and drinks, was Molly. She took in the scene in a split second: Delores, furious and bewildered; her identical twin sister, Clare, looking the picture of wide-eyed innocence; and Jack, standing by, a barely suppressed grin on his face. Molly immediately understood what was happening. *Oh, I see,* she thought, a spark of amusement in her eyes. *Clare is playing Twin Magic. Perfect. Let's help her out.*

She looked at Delores with the same cool, analytical gaze Delores remembered from the dinner. "Oh," Molly said, her voice perfectly neutral. "Hello again." Then, she deliberately shifted her energy, mimicking her sister's more open and proactive demeanor. She turned to Clare, her expression bright and engaging. "Clare, you will not *believe* who I just saw over by the pretzel stand!"

Delores’s brain seemed to short-circuit. She looked from Molly to Clare, then back to Molly. They were identical. Their voices were identical. And now, the one she thought was the quiet, analytical one was acting just as bubbly as the one pretending to be a complete airhead. She couldn't tell them apart at all.

Jack, who could easily see the subtle differences in his cousins' base mannerisms, was thoroughly enjoying watching them get one over on Delores. He decided to play along.

"So, which one of you is driving us home?" Jack asked, looking from one twin to the other with a playful, questioning look, as if he himself couldn't tell.

"I am!" they both said in perfect, cheerful unison.

Delores stared, completely adrift in a sea of identical faces, voices, and now, mannerisms. The ground beneath her feet felt like it had vanished. She had come here to assert control, to manipulate, and instead, she had been rendered utterly powerless by the simple, baffling existence of two people who looked and sounded exactly the same.

"I... you..." she sputtered, her face flushing a deep, embarrassed red.

Clare took the tray from Molly. "Well," she said brightly, turning back to Delores, her act of innocent confusion dropped now that the prank was complete. "It was... interesting to meet you. We should get our table before our food gets cold."

With that, the three of them—Jack flanked by his two identical, smiling cousins—simply walked away, leaving Delores standing alone in the middle of the food court, completely disarmed, utterly humiliated, and finally, blessedly, defeated.

## Chapter 9: Sarah Bennett, Reporting for Duty

### Part 1: A Day of Dread and Duty

The alarm clock on the nightstand blared at 6:15 AM, a harsh, unwelcome sound in the quiet darkness of the bedroom. A groan came from the middle of the queen bed. Jack blindly fumbled for the snooze button, but another hand shot out from beside him and slapped it into silence.

“Time to get up,” Sarah murmured, her voice thick with sleep.

Jack just groaned again, pulling the covers a little tighter. For the past few weeks, ever since the disastrous date, Sarah had been sleeping in bed with him. Her presence was a profound comfort, a solid, safe anchor against the anxiety that had been his constant companion. But this morning, even her closeness couldn’t completely ward off the specific dread he felt for the evening to come: the family dinner at the Peking Duck Inn, where Delores would meet everyone for the first time.

“Come on,” Sarah said, sitting up. “Big day for both of us.”

She was right. They got up and began the quiet, practiced routine of getting ready. Jack pulled on a pair of blue jeans and a fresh, city-issued rec center t-shirt, while Sarah layered comfortable sweatpants and a matching t-shirt over her city-issued swimsuit. She slipped on a pair of flip-flops, her standard work footwear that allowed for a quick transition in case of a water rescue.

As they headed downstairs for breakfast, Sarah, walking a step behind her brother, noticed him pause for a moment at the top of the stairs, his hand gripping the railing a little tighter than usual before he carefully made his way down. It was a subtle sign of his ongoing struggle, a flicker of the physical challenges he faced every day.

They took turns using the bathroom, then Sarah quickly poured two bowls of cereal and two glasses of milk, a simple breakfast for a complicated day. They ate in the quiet of the new kitchen.

“Nervous?” Jack asked.

“A little,” Sarah admitted, swirling the milk in her cereal bowl. “Excited, too. It’s my first time as the acting Aquatics Supervisor for a full day. I just want everything to go right.” She looked at him. “What about you? You’re shadowing Donald for opening and closing procedures today, right?”

Jack nodded, pushing a spoonful of cereal around his bowl. “Yeah. I’m a little nervous about that. It’s a lot of responsibility.” But that wasn’t what was really bothering him. The thought of the dinner that night was a heavy weight in his stomach.

Sarah, ever observant, saw the look on his face. “It’ll be okay, Jack. The dinner, I mean. We’ll all be there. We’re your wall.”

He gave her a small, grateful smile. They finished their breakfast and grabbed their things. At the door leading to the garage, Jack paused and turned to his sister, pulling her into a hug.

“You’re going to be a great boss today,” he said.

“And you’re going to be a great supervisor-in-training,” she replied, hugging him back tightly. “Now let’s go. Don’t want to be late.”

They got into the car, and Jack, as the designated driver for the morning, started the engine and pulled out of the driveway, heading for another fateful day at Fairlawn.

### Part 2: A Sunday Morning at Fairlawn

At 7:30 AM on the dot, Sarah swiped her employee badge, and the side door of the quiet, sleeping building clicked open. In the dim light of the lobby, she and Jack paused.

"Okay," she said, turning to him. "Have a good shift. Try not to miss me too much."

"I'll try," Jack said with a small smile, pulling her into a hug. "You too. Don't be too bossy up there."

"No promises," she replied, hugging him back before heading towards the massive concrete spiral ramp to begin her long day on the second floor. Jack made his way to the downstairs break room in the administration wing to wait for his own 8:45 AM start time.

#### Upstairs: Sarah's Meticulous Routine (7:45 AM)

The moment Sarah clocked in and stepped onto the pool deck, a switch flipped. She was no longer just Jack's sister; she was the acting Aquatics Supervisor for the day, and the vast, 25-meter, 8-lane natatorium was her responsibility. The air was still and humid, the water a perfect, undisturbed sheet of turquoise under the low maintenance lights. She felt a familiar mix of nervous excitement and profound competence. This was her first time in charge, and she was determined to do it right.

She wasn't alone. Two of her most trusted senior lifeguards, Maria and David, clocked in with her, forming her core opening crew.

"Alright, team," Sarah said, her voice clear and confident, echoing slightly in the empty space. "Let's get this place ready for the public. David, you start the deck walk—check all the drains, lane lines, and make sure the rescue equipment is properly placed. Maria, you're on the safety kit and spineboard check. I'm going to run the water chemistry."

Her first task, as always, was the most critical. She moved with a practiced efficiency, taking water samples from multiple points in the massive pool. Back in the aquatics office, she meticulously conducted the tests—chlorine, pH, alkalinity—her movements precise and focused. The familiar, scientific routine was a comfort, a pocket of predictable order in a day that promised to be emotionally complicated. With the levels logged and confirmed to be perfect, she moved on to double-check the pump room readings.

By 8:30 AM, the atmosphere began to change as the next wave of four lifeguards trickled in, their cheerful chatter filling the break room as they got changed and ready for the day. Sarah gathered them all on the pool deck for a pre-shift briefing.

"Morning, everyone," she began, clipboard in hand. "It's Sunday, so expect a heavy family crowd right from the start. Rotations are on the board. Maria, you're on the main stand first. Let's make sure our scanning is sharp today. Stay hydrated, and call for a backup if you need one. Let's have a safe shift."

At 8:45 AM, the final two guards for the morning shift arrived, completing her team. The natatorium lights hummed to life, bringing the room to its full, bright intensity. The water sparkled. At 9:00 AM on the dot, the first rotation of guards were in their stands, rescue tubes across their laps, their gazes already methodically scanning their zones of coverage. Sarah stood on the deck, taking it all in—her team, her pool, her responsibility. With a final, satisfied nod, she picked up her radio.

"Aquatics to Front Desk. The deck is clear. You can open the doors."

#### Downstairs: Jack's Quiet Hour (7:45 AM)

Jack settled into one of the uncomfortable plastic chairs in the empty break room. The silence of the building was a stark contrast to the loud, anxious thoughts churning in his mind. He went through his familiar routine, stowing his lunch in the fridge and filling his water bottle, but the small tasks did little to quell the feeling of dread about the dinner planned for that night. His stomach was in knots.

He sank back into the chair, a bone-deep weariness settling over him that felt heavier than just a poor night's sleep. *Probably just stress,* he thought, rubbing his temples. *Worrying about tonight is draining.* He felt a vague but heavy sense of doom that had been clinging to him for the past day, a feeling that something terrible was on the horizon. *The dinner,* he told himself firmly. *It's just the dinner.*

He pulled a well-worn science fiction paperback from his bag: *The Destruction of Veridia Prime*. He opened it to his bookmarked page, hoping to lose himself in the story. He was at the part where the hero twins, Kael and Lyra, having survived the annihilation of their world and a desperate firefight, had finally collapsed onto the deck of the freighter, the *Stargazer*. He read about their exhaustion, their grief, and their profound relief at finally reaching a safe harbor. The story of their escape resonated deeply, a mirror of his own desperate need to find a sanctuary from Delores. But the words blurred on the page. His mind kept drifting back to her fake, cloying smile and the look of cold fury he’d seen in her eyes.

He sighed, giving up on reading. The anxiety was too much. Instead, he pulled out his MP3 player, slipping on his headphones. He scrolled to one of his favorite bands, Origin, and selected the gentle, reassuring melody of "Wherever You Go". He turned up the volume, letting the song's promise of unwavering loyalty create a protective barrier, a shield against his own churning thoughts. He closed his eyes, completely unaware that he was trying to block out not just his thoughts, but the first, faint, desperate warning signals from his own body.

#### 8:30 AM

Upstairs, the first of Sarah's lifeguards began to arrive. She held a quick, professional briefing on the pool deck, outlining the rotation schedule and reminding them to be extra vigilant on a busy Sunday. Her voice was confident and clear, the perfect picture of a supervisor in command.

Downstairs, at 8:30 AM, Jack saw Donald, his shift supervisor for the day, walk into the break room and give him a friendly nod. It was almost time. He took off his headphones, the comforting melody of Origin's "Wherever You Go" fading away, and mentally prepared himself for the social demands of the front desk. The last few minutes of his quiet solitude ticked away. The dread about the evening's dinner remained, a cold stone in the pit of his stomach.

At precisely 8:45 AM, Jack clocked in. Donald met him by the main office door. “Ready for your crash course in opening procedures, Bennett?”

“Ready as I’ll ever be,” Jack replied.

Donald led him to the office safe. “Okay, first things first. We take out all the bags. Change Purse, and registers one through four. They all get counted, every day, no exceptions.”

They laid the five canvas bags on the counter. “Each one needs to have exactly one hundred dollars to start,” Donald explained, his movements practiced and efficient. “Bags one through four should have fifty in ones and fifty in fives, if you can. Makes breaking twenties easier.” He opened the Change Purse and frowned. “Well, that’s not ideal. Just five twenties. Someone on closing shift last night was lazy. Means we’ll have no way of breaking larger bills until we build up some change. We'll have to ask patrons for smaller bills if we can.”

For the next ten minutes, Donald walked Jack through the meticulous process. They counted and verified each bag. Donald then had Jack sign the logbook under his own signature, a small but significant act of trusting him with the responsibility. They returned the Change Purse and the spare Bag 4 to the safe, then headed out to the front desk with the other three bags, loading the cash drawers into the three registers.

“Alright, last step before we open,” Donald said, pointing to the two large monitors mounted behind the desk. He showed Jack how to turn them on and load the day’s event schedule. The screens flickered to life, displaying the day's events: a child's birthday party in Room 1, a baby shower in the combined Rooms 3 & 4, and a "Pool Blast" party in the combined Rooms 5 & 6.

At 8:59 AM, Sarah’s voice came over the radio, crisp and professional. "Aquatics to Front Desk. The deck is clear. You can open the doors."

Jack, standing near the main radio, picked up the receiver. “10-4, Aquatics. Front desk is ready. Facility Supervisor is opening the doors now.”

The day began. The usual Sunday morning crowd flowed in. At 9:00 AM sharp, Mandy arrived, smiling a quick good morning before heading to the middle register to count her drawer. Donald took his position at the register on the right, next to the Scan Station, and Jack settled in at his spot at the Scan Station, the familiar rhythm of the job a fragile shield against his inner turmoil.

### Part 3: A Tale of Two Siblings

The clock on the wall read 9:00 AM sharp. The doors of Fairlawn Recreation Center slid open, and the Sunday morning rush began.

#### Downstairs: Jack at the Scan Station

Jack took his position at the Scan Station, a familiar post he knew well. To his right, the three cash registers were staffed for the morning rush: Donald (next to Jack) on the right register, then Mandy next to Donald at the middle register. The morning unfolded in a familiar, rhythmic blur. The first hour flew by in a wave of families arriving for the Sunday morning Open Swim and groups of adults heading to the gymnasium. Jack greeted each one, his hands and mouth on autopilot while his mind replayed the conversation with Sarah in the car. The vague sense of doom he’d felt all morning was a low, persistent hum beneath the surface of his professional calm. He overheard a group of teenagers complaining to Mandy at the register. "What do you mean there's no basketball?" one of them grumbled. Mandy just offered a polite, practiced smile. "I'm sorry, we haven't had open gym basketball on Sundays for over a year now. We have volleyball and badminton available until this afternoon. The equipment is in the gym." The teens grumbled but headed towards the gym, a scene Jack and the other front desk staff were all too familiar with.

#### Upstairs: Sarah in the Natatorium

As the acting Aquatics Supervisor, Sarah commanded the pool deck. The vast, 8-lane, 25-meter natatorium was her domain, and she moved through it with a quiet, confident authority. With the pool now open to the public for

Open Swim, the deck was a cheerful chaos of splashing children and watchful parents. Sarah wasn't in one of the high stands; she was on the deck, her eyes constantly scanning, not just her zone, but her entire team's. She made a subtle hand signal to a younger lifeguard, reminding him to keep his head on a swivel. Her leadership was quiet but absolute. She had seen how tired Jack looked that morning, and it bothered her more than she let on.

#### 10:30 AM

The initial rushes began to subside, leaving a steady hum of activity. Downstairs, Jack felt a wave of dizziness as he stood up to stretch his back, leaning heavily on the counter for a moment. Upstairs, Sarah was walking the perimeter of the pool, her focus absolute, a necessary shield against the worry for her brother that still lingered at the back of her mind.

### Part 4: A Shared Break

The morning rush at Fairlawn eventually gave way to the steadier, calmer rhythm of a Sunday afternoon. At 1:23 PM, during a quiet lull, the front desk radio crackled to life, the single channel shared by the whole facility carrying Sarah's familiar voice. "Aquatics to Front Desk."

Donald, the acting supervisor, picked up the receiver. "Go for the desk."

"Hey, Donald," Sarah's voice came through, "I'm about to take my thirty-minute lunch. Is Jack available to join me?"

Donald glanced at Jack, who nodded eagerly. It was a familiar routine for the siblings to try and coordinate their breaks when they could. "Sure, he can join you," Donald replied into the radio. "We're not that busy. He's all yours."

With his supervisor's approval, Jack then picked up the radio himself. "Hey, Sarah," he said, his voice a little weary, "can you meet me downstairs in the main break room? I'm feeling a bit exhausted."

There was no hesitation. "10-4, Jack," she replied, the professional radio lingo a familiar comfort. "I'm on my way."

A few minutes later, Sarah emerged from the second-floor ramp. As she approached the desk, Jack caught Donald's eye and gestured that he was taking his break. They walked to the time clock together, clocking out at 1:30 PM on the dot for their unpaid break.

In the break room, Jack sank into one of the chairs with a heavy sigh, pulling out the lunch he had packed that morning. Sarah sat across from him, her own lunch in hand, and for a few moments, they ate in a comfortable, familiar silence. It was Sarah who noticed it first. Her brother, who had seemed merely anxious and preoccupied that morning, now looked utterly, profoundly exhausted. There were dark circles under his eyes that hadn't been there a few hours ago, and his movements were slow, as if his limbs were weighted down.

“You okay, Jack?” she asked, her voice soft with concern. “You look completely wiped out.”

“I’m fine,” he said, but the words lacked conviction. “Just… really tired today, I guess.” He paused, looking at his sister. “And I just feel… weird. Anxious. I can't shake it.”

Sarah’s expression softened with empathy. Of course he was anxious. They both were. “It’s Delores, isn’t it?” she said, her voice full of understanding. “This whole situation is a mess. It’s completely normal that you’d be feeling anxious and drained before the dinner tonight.”

Jack nodded, latching onto the logical explanation. It had to be Delores. The stress was just getting to him.

Just then, Jean, the young part-time cashier, came into the break room to grab a drink from the vending machine. She offered them a shy smile. "Hey, you two." As she passed their table, she also noticed the deep weariness on Jack's face. He looked pale, almost grey, under the fluorescent lights. Her smile faltered into a look of concern, a silent echo of Sarah's own worry. She didn't say anything, not wanting to intrude, but she made a mental note. Jack did not look well at all.

Sarah watched her brother take a slow, weary bite of his sandwich. Her protective instincts, always on high alert, were now humming with a low-grade anxiety. His exhaustion seemed to go deeper than just a long week.

“Hey,” she said, making a decision. “I know you were going to drive us to dinner tonight, but you look like you could fall asleep right here. How about I drive instead?”

A wave of profound relief washed over Jack’s face. “Yeah?” he asked, his voice full of gratitude. “That… that would be great, actually. Thanks, Sarah.”

“Of course,” she said, her concern deepening. He had agreed without any of his usual token protests. He was really, truly, spent. They finished the rest of their thirty-minute break, the unspoken worry hanging in the air between them. They clocked back in together and walked back towards the front desk. Sarah gave his arm a final, reassuring squeeze before heading back up the ramp to the pool deck, leaving Jack to finish the last few hours of his shift, unaware that his body was already in the midst of a quiet, desperate battle.

### Part 5: Trial by Fecal Contamination

At 2:00 PM, Sarah returned to the humid warmth of the natatorium, feeling a little more settled after the quiet lunch with Jack. Her shift was halfway over, and everything was running smoothly on her first day as the acting Aquatics Supervisor. She began her rounds, checking in with the lifeguards on the stands.

At 2:04 PM, the entire facility's shared radio channel crackled to life, the sound sharp and urgent. "Stand Three to Sarah! We have a Code Brown in the shallow end! Code Brown!"

Downstairs at the front desk, Jack, Donald, and Jean all looked up from their tasks, their expressions instantly serious. They knew what that meant.

A moment later, a second, more panicked voice erupted from the radio. "Stand Two to Sarah! We have a Code Yellow at the source of the Code Brown! Repeat, we have a Code Yellow as well!"

Upstairs, Sarah’s Head Lifeguard training kicked into high gear. She was already moving, her voice ringing with a calm authority that cut through the chaos. "Everybody out of the water!" she commanded. "The pool is now closed!"

She quickly made her way to the source of the problem. A young child had gotten sick, and it was a worst-case scenario: a simultaneous vomit and diarrhea incident. After a quick assessment, she knew this wasn't a one-hour problem. This was a "shut it down" problem, requiring a full hyperchlorination process that would take over 12 hours.

She picked up her own radio, her voice firm and decisive, formalizing what the front desk already suspected. "Aquatics to Front Desk."

A familiar, steady voice came back instantly. "Go for the desk, Sarah. It's Jack. We heard the calls. What's the official word?"

Relief washed over her for a fraction of a second. "Jack, be advised, the natatorium is closed for the remainder of the day due to a major fecal incident. I repeat, the pool is closed for the rest of the day."

"10-4," Jack's voice replied, all business. He immediately relayed the message to Donald and Jean, who began the process of informing patrons and placing "Pool Closed" signs. Then, per city policy, Jack picked up the desk phone. He first called the Windsor Woods Recreation Center, then the Red Mill Recreation Center—the two facilities closest to Fairlawn. He quickly informed their front desks of the closure, knowing they would appreciate the heads-up so they wouldn't send any patrons looking for a pool their way. It was a proactive, considerate move, a small act of support for his sister on a very difficult day.

Upstairs, Sarah turned to face the line of disappointed patrons. To her profound relief, the grumbling was minimal. The patrons were disappointed, certainly, but they were also understanding. They recognized that safety had to come first, and none of them were angry at Sarah or her lifeguards for making the correct call.

It was a true trial by fire on her first day as acting supervisor, and she had met it head-on. With the public cleared, Sarah and her team spent the next two hours on the grueling decontamination process. Once the chemicals were in, their active work was done. She gathered her lifeguards in the aquatics office.

"Alright, team," she said, her voice full of the calm authority she had projected all day. "There's nothing more we can do up here. The pool's down for the count. For those of you who want to go home early, you're dismissed for the day. If anyone needs to finish their hours, I'll call the other centers and find a place for you."

### Part 6: The Longest Shift

With the public cleared from the natatorium, Sarah and her team spent the next two hours on the grueling decontamination process. They skimmed, treated, and shocked the pool with high levels of chlorine, following the city's health and safety protocols to the letter. By around 4:00 PM, they had done everything they could. Now, all that was left was to let the powerful filtration system cycle the treated water overnight.

Having managed her staff—dismissing those who wanted to go home early and finding shifts at other centers for those who needed the hours—Sarah had one last supervisory duty. She headed downstairs to the administration wing to debrief the acting Facility Supervisor, Rebecca, a friendly Event Coordinator from Rec Services who had been assigned the role for the day. Together, they meticulously filled out the incident report for Sarah's boss, Scott, detailing the time, the nature of the contamination, and the precise steps taken for remediation.

At 4:30 PM, the report was finished. With a half-hour left on her own shift, Sarah felt a powerful pull to be near her brother. She walked out to the front lobby and pulled up a spare stool behind the counter.
