"Pool's all treated," she announced to Donald as she settled in. "Thought I'd come hang out down here until close. Be an extra set of hands."

"Happy to have you," Donald said with a smile.

She spent the rest of the afternoon there, a quiet, supportive presence. She watched Jack as he greeted patrons, his face a pale mask of polite professionalism. She could see the deep weariness in his eyes, the same exhaustion from their lunch break, and her own anxiety simmered just below the surface. She was glad she was here, close by, keeping a watchful, loving eye on the brother she knew was still struggling with a sense of doom, an anxiety she hoped would vanish the moment they finally dealt with Delores for good.

At 4:45 PM, Jean’s voice came over the PA system, her tone clear and professional: "May I have your attention please: the time is now 4:45 PM and Fairlawn Recreation Center will be closing in 15 minutes. If you need to make arrangements for a ride, please do so at this time. Again, the time is now 4:45 PM and Fairlawn Recreation Center will be closing in 15 minutes. If you need to make arrangements for a ride, please do so at this time.”

As the last few patrons began to gather their things, Donald started the initial closing procedures for the registers. At 5:00 PM on the dot, Jack, as part of his training, made the final closing announcement over the PA system. While Donald locked the main doors, Jean, her shift now over, was dismissed for the day.

“Alright, Jack,” Donald said, “time for the final lesson of the day: closing report.”

He showed Jack how to count down his own drawer, then how to consolidate the day's total cash and check intake. Together, they filled out the closing report, and Donald had Jack accompany him to the safe to watch as he signed the logbook, a final act of passing on the responsibility.

At 5:06 PM, the report was filed and the safe was locked. “That’s us,” Donald said. “You’re all set, Jack. Go on home.”

He met Sarah, who had been waiting patiently after being officially dismissed by Rebecca a few moments earlier. They walked to the time clock together, and they both clocked out at 5:12 PM. As far as the city's payroll system was concerned, with its rounding to the nearest quarter-hour, they had both finished at 5:15 PM.

They walked out to the car together, the chilly February air a welcome change. True to her word, Sarah got into the driver's seat. She drove them away from the rec center and towards the Peking Duck Inn, where the rest of their family was already waiting. As they pulled into the bustling parking lot, they saw Delores getting out of her mother's car, her face a mask of polite neutrality. The stage was set for the first, and final, meeting between Delores and the Bennett clan.

### Part 7: Peking Duck Inn

The Peking Duck Inn was a riot of festive color and cheerful noise on Sunday, February 11th. Simon, the owner, had decorated the day before for the upcoming Chinese New Year, and vibrant red lanterns and gold good luck symbols hung from the ceiling, casting a warm, celebratory glow over the bustling dining room.

The entire Bennett clan arrived together after Jack and Sarah’s long shift at the rec center. It was the first time Jermaine, Devin, and Sam would meet Delores. Simon, a kind man in his late 50s with a warm smile and a hint of a British Hong Kong accent, greeted them at the door like family.

"Jermaine! Devin! Sam! So good to see you all!" he boomed, before his eyes fell on Jack. "And Jack, my boy, you look tired! Long day at the rec?"

"You know it, Simon," Jack said with a weary smile. He then gestured to the young woman standing awkwardly beside him. "Simon, this is my girlfriend, Delores. Delores, this is our good friend, Simon. He owns the place."

"A pleasure to meet you, Delores," Simon said with a polite, professional smile, though his experienced gaze took in the subtle tension around her. He then led them to a pair of rectangular tables he had already pushed together in the center of the room.

The seating arrangement was a subtle, coordinated dance. The four cousins took one side, with Jack and Sarah in the middle, flanked by Molly and Clare. On the other side, Jermaine sat in the middle of his father and brother. This left one open seat, directly across from Molly, for Delores. The family had their analyst in the perfect position to observe.

Simon returned to the table, his pen and pad ready. "The usual for everyone?" he asked the Bennett clan.

"You know it, Simon," Jermaine said with a grateful smile.

Simon then turned to Delores, a single, elegant menu in his hand. "And for you, miss? Since it's your first time here, please, take a look."

Delores just stared, bewildered. *Doesn't he have menus for them?* she thought, looking at the family as they all nodded in agreement. *How do they even know what they want?*

The family conversation flowed freely as they waited for their food, a comfortable and chaotic mix of work stories, jokes about the upcoming move, and plans for Sarah's birthday. Delores sat in a pocket of resentful silence, a clear outsider to their easy intimacy.

She shifted constantly in her chair, the hard wood of the seat aggravating the dull, heavy ache that had started in her lower back an hour ago. She crossed and uncrossed her legs, trying to find a position that didn't make her want to scream. She felt bloated, cramped, and cornered.

It was Devin who unintentionally lit the fuse, trying to be polite. "So, Delores," he asked, "Jack mentioned you’re a student at VDU. Do you have any siblings?"

Delores froze, a flicker of something dark and panicked in her eyes. A sharp cramp seized her abdomen, forcing a grimace she tried to hide behind her water glass. "No," she said, a little too quickly. "I'm an only child."

The lie was sharp and sudden, but plausible enough. However, a few moments later, Jermaine, trying again to include her, asked, "So growing up, was it just you and your parents then?"

The physical pain flared again, syncing perfectly with her irritation at the intrusive questions. "No!" Delores snapped, the word exploding out of her louder than she intended as she shifted abruptly to relieve the pressure.

"I mean, yes. It's just... it's complicated. It’s hard when you have people who are always interfering, always judging you, like my last boyfriend's precious sisters, or... or like..." She stopped abruptly, her eyes wide.

She had been about to say, "...or like my brother," a reference to Dan, whose existence she had just denied. She had come dangerously close to revealing the entire pattern—her resentment of Leo's sisters, the existence of Dan, her history of targeting men with close female relatives. She had almost spilled her entire dirty secret right there on the table.

She caught herself, a flush rising on her cheeks. "I mean... my last relationship was just very difficult," she finished lamely.

A profound, telling silence fell over the Bennett side of the table. Every single one of them—from Sam's wise, old eyes to Molly's sharp, analytical gaze—had heard it. The slip. The raw, venomous anger in her voice when she mentioned "precious sisters." The way she had stumbled, catching herself just before revealing too much. They didn't know the name Leo Porter, they didn't know about Dan, but they all recognized the sound of a carefully constructed lie falling apart. It was a massive, collective red flag.

Molly, watching from directly across the table, had noticed the physical tell, too. The wince before the snap. The constant shifting. *She’s uncomfortable,* Molly noted, filing the data away. *She's in pain, and she's losing control of her temper because of it.*

The conversation after that was polite, but the dynamic had irrevocably shifted. The family continued to talk freely amongst themselves, their laughter and easy banter a wall that Delores could not penetrate. When they did address her, their questions were clipped, their responses short. Delores could feel it. She was on very, very thin ice, and she had no one to blame but herself.

### Part 8: A Dinner on Thin Ice

Simon returned from the kitchen a few minutes later, the wonderful aroma of the food preceding him. He placed a steaming bowl of wonton soup and a plate of crispy shrimp egg rolls in front of Jack, a familiar, comforting sight. He then served the other Bennetts their own "usuals" before setting a plate of what Delores had ordered in front of her.

"Enjoy, everyone," Simon said with a warm, genuine smile for the family and a polite, professional nod for their guest. He then engaged in a bit of quick, easy banter with Jermaine and Sam, a comfortable back-and-forth that was full of shared history and in-jokes that Delores couldn't possibly follow. She just watched, a tight, forced smile on her face, feeling more like an outsider than ever.

As the family began to eat, the conversation flowed freely again, but Delores barely touched her food. She was on high alert, acutely aware that she was on very thin ice after her earlier outburst. Her mind was a chaotic jumble. She had to be polite, had to be charming, but the sight of Molly and Clare, sitting side-by-side in their identical outfits, was deeply unsettling. She remembered the mall, the dizzying confusion of the "Twin Magic" prank, and a fresh wave of resentment and frustration washed over her.

She struggled to keep her composure, forcing herself to take small, meaningless bites of her food, pecking at it like a bird. But it wasn't just the social tension. Every few minutes, a subtle, sharp grimace would cross her face, and she would shift her weight in the chair, looking physically uncomfortable. At one point, she stopped eating entirely, her hand going instinctively to her lower abdomen, pressing firmly against her waistband as she squeezed her eyes shut for a second.

Molly, ever the analyst, noticed. Seated directly across from Delores, she had a perfect view of the other woman's tense posture and untouched plate. She watched Delores wince again, her knuckles white as she gripped her napkin under the table.

She's not eating, Molly thought, logging another mental note. And she’s in pain.

Molly observed the sudden pallor in Delores's cheeks and the way her irritability seemed to spike every time she shifted in her seat. The pieces clicked into place—the short fuse earlier, the snapping at Jermaine, the physical discomfort now.

Mental Note \#6: She’s starting her period. And judging by the look on her face, it was a bad one.

Molly took a sip of her tea, keeping her expression neutral. It wasn't exactly polite dinner conversation to point out that their guest was dealing with menstrual cramps, especially with her dad and uncle right there. She kept the observation to herself, but it added a layer of context to the evening. Delores wasn't just mean; she was miserable.

Delores was caught in a trap of her own making. She had to be the perfect, polite girlfriend in front of Jack's father, uncle, and grandfather, but the very presence of his happy, close-knit family, especially the identical twin cousins who had so recently humiliated her, was a constant, grating irritant. The festive, happy atmosphere of the restaurant only served to highlight her own profound sense of isolation and anger.

### Part 9: The Aftermath

The dinner ended with a polite, strained quiet. When the check arrived, Grandpa Sam simply placed his credit card on the tray without a word. The rest of the family maintained their masks of civility, but underneath, a collective, silent animosity for Delores had solidified. They hated the way she made Jack tense, but they also respected his agency as an adult; he had a plan, and they would be his "wall" when he chose to execute it.

Outside, in the chilly night air of the parking lot, Delores's mother was waiting. As the family said their goodbyes, Jack, needing the reassurance of his safe harbor, gave Sarah a tight hug, followed by equally warm hugs for Molly and Clare. Delores, standing by her mother’s car, watched the easy, physical affection, her face a mask of polite indifference. Internally, however, she was seething. *Look at them,* she thought, her mind twisting their innocent comfort into a personal affront. *All over him. It's not normal. He doesn't need them when he has me.* She forced a bright, false smile as Jack approached for a stiff, obligatory goodbye hug.

As Delores was about to get into her mother's car, she heard Jack's voice, clear and honest, carry across the quiet parking lot. He looked directly at his sister.

"Sarah?" he asked, his voice full of the exhaustion and anxiety from the evening. "After tonight... I really don't want to be alone. Will you please sleep in my bed with me tonight?"

Delores froze, her hand on the car door, listening.

Sarah didn't hesitate for a second. She put a comforting hand on her brother's arm. "Yes, Jack," she said, her voice a warm, unwavering promise. "Absolutely. Of course, I will."

The words hit Delores like a physical blow. It wasn't just a hug. It was a direct, verbal request for a level of intimacy and comfort that completely excluded her. He wasn't just tolerating his sister; he was actively choosing her, seeking her out for the night. The anger that had been simmering all evening boiled over into a silent, venomous rage. She yanked the car door open and slid into the seat, slamming the door shut behind her, the sound sharp in the quiet night. She didn't look back as her mother drove away, but her mind was a storm of jealousy and fury. The wall Jack's family had built around him was higher and stronger than she had ever imagined.

### Part 10: A Safe Harbor at Home

The drive back from the Peking Duck Inn was quiet. The festive atmosphere of the restaurant, a place that usually felt like a second home, had been soured by Delores’s tense, resentful presence. Back at the house, the three of them—Jermaine, Sarah, and Jack—retreated to the living room, the unspoken weight of the evening settling around them.

Jack sank into the corner of the sofa, looking pale and utterly drained. Sarah immediately sat beside him, pulling him into a tight, protective hug. He leaned into her, his head resting on her shoulder, a shudder running through him.

"I'm scared of her, Sarah," he finally admitted, his voice a muffled whisper against her shirt. "The way she looked at me… at all of us. I’m really scared."

"I know, Jack. We all saw it," she said, her arms tightening around him. "But you're not alone in this. We won't let her hurt you."

Jermaine, who had been watching them from his armchair, leaned forward, his expression full of a father's fierce, gentle love. "Son, listen to me," he said, his voice a comforting rumble. "I know tonight was hard. But I want you to remember what we're building. In just a couple of weeks, we'll be in the new house. You'll have the Quad, that whole amazing space you helped design. You, Sarah, Molly, and Clare, all together."

Jack considered the plan. The Quad. A sanctuary built for them, by them. It would give him all the safe, non-sexual female contact he could ever want, with his three most favorite women in the world. He thought of their easy hugs, their shared laughter, the uncomplicated comfort of their presence. That was safety. That was home.

And yet, Delores represented something different, something he thought he was supposed to want, something he could never receive from his sister or his cousins: romantic affection. But after tonight, after seeing the coldness in her eyes, the possessiveness, the barely concealed rage… was this what he really wanted? The contrast between Delores's toxic intensity and the pure, unconditional love he felt from his family was a chasm.

A wave of profound exhaustion, deeper than anything he'd felt before, washed over him, making it hard to even think. "I… I think I need to go to bed," he mumbled, looking at Sarah. It was only 7:22 PM, far earlier than his usual 9:30 bedtime, but the emotional and physical toll of the day had completely spent him.

"Okay, Jack. Let's get you to bed," Sarah said immediately, her voice soft with concern.

She helped him up from the sofa and followed him into the bedroom that had, over the past few weeks, become their shared space. As Jack began to get ready for bed, Sarah noticed he was so exhausted that his movements were clumsy, his hands struggling with the buttons on his shirt. She made a quiet mental note of his profound fatigue as she stepped in to help him get changed into his pajamas, her worry for him a sharp, persistent ache.

A few minutes later, they were both in the queen bed. Jack rolled onto his side, his back to her, a silent indication of his vulnerability. Sarah moved in behind him, wrapping her arm around his waist and pulling him into a secure, comforting embrace, spooning him just as she had on other difficult nights.

"Try to rest," she whispered into his ear. "And remember the promise of the Quad. A place with all of us. A place where you're safe. You could never, ever have that with Delores."

Jack didn't respond, but she felt the tension in his shoulders begin to release as he leaned back into her embrace. She held him, her own heart heavy with worry, and together, in the quiet darkness of the room, they both slowly, finally, drifted off to sleep.

## Chapter 10: Confusion and Stress

### Part 1: A Morning Shrouded in Fog 🥣

The morning of Monday, February 12th, arrived not with the usual cheerful chaos of their shared home, but with a quiet, heavy stillness. Sarah woke first, a habit born from years of being her brother's protector. She turned her head on the pillow in the

full-sized bed they now shared and looked at Jack. He was still asleep, but it wasn't a restful sleep. His breathing was shallow, and his brow was furrowed, as if he were wrestling with something even in his dreams. The restless, anxious night had taken its toll on both of them.

"Jack," she said softly, gently shaking his shoulder. "Time to get up. We have that stats class this morning."

He groaned, the sound muffled by his pillow. It took him a long, weary moment to push himself into a sitting position. As he reached for the jeans and t-shirt laid out for him, Sarah noticed his movements were clumsy and slow. He managed the jeans, but when he picked up his shirt, his fingers fumbled with the buttons, unable to guide them through the holes. His mild cerebral palsy always seemed more pronounced when he was exhausted, but this was different. His hands were trembling slightly.

"Here, let me," Sarah said, her voice even and matter-of-fact. She moved in front of him and quickly buttoned his shirt, her own anxiety simmering just below the surface.

This whole thing with Delores is tearing him apart, she thought, her worry solidifying.

Downstairs in the kitchen, their father, Jermaine, was already at the table, sipping a cup of coffee and looking through the morning paper. He looked up as they entered, his smile fading slightly as he took in his son's appearance.

Jack walked over to the refrigerator and reached for the gallon of milk, but his grip was unsteady. The heavy container wobbled precariously in his hand.

"I've got it," Sarah said, quickly taking it from him before he could drop it. She placed two bowls on the counter and grabbed his favorite cereal from the pantry. She poured a bowl for him and one for herself, then added the milk, performing the simple task he’d done for himself a thousand times before.

"You alright there, son?" Jermaine asked, his voice laced with concern. "You look completely wiped out".

Jack just shrugged, sinking into a chair at the table. "Just tired," he mumbled.

He stared down at his favorite cereal, picked up his spoon, and began pushing the colorful loops around in the milk in slow, swirling patterns. He didn't take a single bite.

Jermaine caught Sarah's eye, his expression grim. "That dinner last night really did a number on him," he said in a low voice, certain he was identifying the source of his son's distress.

Sarah nodded, her own face tight with worry. "I know, Dad. He barely slept. The stress of that girl... it's just eating him alive". They both watched Jack poke at his untouched breakfast, their shared worry settling between them like a heavy weight. For them, the conclusion was as logical as it was obvious: the ugly confrontation with Delores was taking a severe toll on him, and they would have to help him through it.

### Part 2: A Day of Confusion (Monday, February 12<sup>th</sup>, 2007)

The day after the disastrous dinner at the Peking Duck Inn, the entire family was on high alert. The morning routine was quiet, a stark contrast to the usual cheerful chaos. Jack was withdrawn, the profound exhaustion from the weekend clinging to him like a shroud. He picked at his breakfast, his appetite gone, a symptom Molly and Sarah exchanged a worried look over. They both attributed his lack of hunger to the immense stress of the Delores situation .

At the rec center, the day was a struggle. It was a short-staffed Monday, with just Jack on a register and Jean at the Scan Station, while Kaitlyn, Jennifer’s assistant, stayed in the back office, observing how Jack handled the responsibility of running the desk largely on his own. Jack, usually so patient and steady, found himself easily irritated by the normal, everyday demands of the front desk. The constant phone ringing, the cheerful chatter of patrons, the bright fluorescent lights—it was all a low-grade sensory assault that left his nerves feeling raw and exposed. He was clumsy, dropping a stack of registration forms and fumbling with the cash drawer, his fine motor skills seemingly betraying him. Everyone assumed he was just reeling from the awful family dinner.

That evening, the family gathered, their conversation circling around how to best support Jack in the upcoming, inevitable breakup. In the middle of their discussion, Jack's phone rang. His face went pale when he saw the caller ID. It was Delores.

He retreated to his and Sarah's shared bedroom for a semblance of privacy. "Hello?" he answered, his voice barely a whisper.

"Hey," Delores's voice was a purr on the other end, completely ignoring the tension from the previous night. "I was just thinking... with Valentine's Day coming up on Wednesday, we should do something really special. Just us. No family." Her words were carefully chosen, a gentle poison. "I found this romantic little Italian place. We could have a proper date, just the two of us, and really connect, you know?"

The suggestion caught Jack completely off guard. A fancy Italian dinner? This was the girl who seemed to subsist on VDU cafeteria food and whose financial situation was, to put it mildly, precarious. The gesture was so out of character that it could only be a performance, a grand, manipulative attempt to love-bomb him.

His stomach churned. It was a test. A clear attempt to isolate him from the protective "wall" of his family. But he was trapped. He hadn't executed the breakup plan yet, and to refuse a Valentine's Day date would surely provoke a fight he didn't have the energy for.

"Okay," he heard himself say, the word tasting like ash. "Yeah, that sounds... nice."

He hung up, the feeling of impending doom now a suffocating certainty. He had just agreed to walk into the lion's den, alone. When he told his sister and cousins, their faces were a mixture of fury and concern, but they understood his predicament. The plan shifted: he would endure the Valentine's dinner, and they would be his immediate backup, ready for his call the second it was over .

That night, sleep offered no escape. He was plagued by restless anxiety, waking up several times in a cold sweat, the feeling that something terrible was about to happen so potent it felt like a physical presence in the room. Sarah, sleeping beside him, would murmur his name, and he would focus on the sound of her steady breathing, the only thing that could anchor him in the terrifying darkness.

### Part 3: A Misfired Warning

It was Tuesday afternoon, February 13th. The Fairlawn lobby was a cacophony of after-school energy. The gymnasium was filled with the thud of basketballs from Open Gym, a group fitness class was blasting upbeat music from a meeting room, and a steady stream of patrons chattered as they checked in. At the front desk, Mandy was the acting supervisor, while Jean and Susan handled the registers.

Jack was at the Scan Station, trying to focus. But today, the familiar sounds of the rec center felt different. They were sharp, grating, and overwhelming. The beep of his scanner felt like a nail being driven into his skull. The overhead fluorescent lights seemed to hum at a painful frequency, making him squint. He felt a profound, bone-deep weariness, and the low-grade sense of impending doom that had been his constant companion for days was now a loud, screaming alarm in his mind.

He saw Mandy, his coworker, wave to get his attention from her register, but her voice was lost in the overwhelming noise. He just shook his head, unable to process her question, and pressed the heels of his hands against his temples for a second. It wasn't enough. The sensory assault was too much, and his carefully constructed composure finally cracked. A choked sob escaped his throat, and hot, heavy tears began to track down his face as he crumbled, utterly overwhelmed.

Mandy saw it instantly. She recognized the signs of a full autistic meltdown—the flinching, the pained expression, and now, the uncontrollable tears. *It's that awful girl,* she thought, her heart aching with sympathy for him. *He's so stressed about her, he's at his breaking point.* Her first instinct, born of her secret crush, was to rush over and comfort him herself. She had seen the easy physical closeness he shared with his sister and yearned to offer that same comfort. But she immediately pushed the thought aside. This wasn't about her feelings. This was about Jack's needs, and she knew perfectly well that the person he needed right now was Sarah.

She grabbed the radio handset. "Front Desk to Aquatics," she said, her voice clear and urgent. "We have a Code Purple at the Front Desk."

Upstairs, on the pool deck, Sarah heard the call crackle over the radio she wore on her hip. Her blood ran cold. She didn't hesitate. She signaled to the next senior lifeguard. "You have the deck," she commanded, and was already moving, her wet flip-flops slapping against the tile as she ran for the ramp.

She arrived in the lobby moments later to see Jack sobbing at the Scan Station, with Mandy, Susan, and Jean forming a quiet, protective screen around him. Sarah immediately put herself between him and the flow of patrons, creating a physical barrier.

"Jack," she said, her voice low and steady. "I'm here. Let's go to the break room. Now."

He just nodded, unable to speak, and allowed her to guide him away from the noise and the lights, into the quiet sanctuary of the empty break room. As the door closed behind them, he collapsed into a chair, his body wracked with deep, tearing sobs. Sarah was there in an instant, wrapping her arms around him in a tight, grounding hug, her hand rubbing firm, steady circles on his back, desperately trying to calm him.

A moment later, the door opened quietly. It was Jennifer, his main supervisor, her face etched with concern. She saw Sarah holding her sobbing brother, assessed that the situation was being handled, and gave a single, supportive nod. "Take all the time you need, you two," she said softly, before closing the door, leaving them in privacy.

After a few more minutes, under the steady pressure of his sister's embrace, Jack's meltdown began to subside. His sobs lessened into shuddering breaths. He finally pulled back, wiping his eyes, looking utterly spent. "I'm sorry," he whispered. "It was just… too much. Too loud."

"It's okay," Sarah said, rubbing his back. "It's the stress, Jack. This whole thing with Delores is tearing you apart. But it's almost over. We'll deal with her, and this will stop."

He took a few more deep breaths, his body still trembling, but the worst of the storm had passed. "I think... I think I can go back out," he said, wanting the normalcy of his routine.

Sarah looked at him, her heart aching, but she respected his resolve. She gave him one last, tight hug. "Okay," she said. "Call me if you need me. For anything." She watched him leave the break room, then turned and headed back up the ramp to resume her own duties, the image of her brother's pale, tear-streaked face burned into her mind.

## Chapter 11: Welcome to Work

### Part 1: Off to Work

Jack woke up slowly, the grogginess of sleep giving way to a dull, anxious weight in his chest. For a moment, he didn't know where he was. Then he felt a gentle, steady breathing beside him and remembered. He was in his own bed, and Sarah was there, a solid, comforting presence beside him. The memory of the the creepy interrogation, Delores’s manipulative tears, and the horrifying accuracy of Molly’s theory—came flooding back. He was grateful he hadn't been alone.

They got ready for work in a quiet, shared rhythm. The new normal of their shared space was unspoken but understood. Sarah drove them to Fairlawn, the Stark Paladin a familiar bubble of safety against the world.

As she pulled into a far-off parking spot, she glanced at her brother. He had been quiet the whole ride, staring out the window.

“Hey,” she said softly. “You with me?”

Jack took a deep breath. “The other night… hearing what you guys thought… it made it all real. I want to end it, Sarah. I really do. But I’m scared of what would happen. I'm scared of *her*.”

“I know,” Sarah said, her voice full of a grim understanding. “After what you and Molly told us, we’re all scared for you. She's not just a creep, Jack. She's a predator. We saw how she reacted to Molly just hugging you. She has a huge problem with anyone being close to you.”

“So what do I do?” he asked, his voice small.

“For today, we just get through work,” Sarah said, her tone firm and reassuring as they got out of the car. “Tonight, we'll make a solid plan with Molly and Clare. All of us together. We’re your wall, remember?”

“Agreed,” Jack said, a little of the tension leaving his shoulders. “Just us. No more her.” He offered his sister a hug before they headed for the door, a gesture of gratitude and solidarity.

Jack had about fifteen minutes before his 9:00 AM shift. He put his lunch in the break room fridge after greeting his boss. Sarah, with a long wait until her 10:30 AM shift, headed to the aquatics office to stow her things and check the pool’s chemical balance logs. Jack made his way to the restroom, then filled his water bottle. The usual things.

He clocked in and made his way to the front desk to begin his day. He was assigned the scan station for now, with plans to be put on a register later when another worker arrived. Just another typical day at the front desk.

He started greeting patrons as he always did, his voice polite and steady, soon settling into the familiar routine that had worked well for him for the past year and a half. Nothing out of the ordinary on the surface, but underneath, he was counting the hours until he could be with his family again, safe and away from the threat that now had a name and a face.

### Part 2: Morning at Fairlawn

The clock on the wall read 9:00 AM sharp. Jack took his position at the scan station, a familiar post he knew well. To his left, the three cash registers were staffed for the morning rush: Susan, Mandy, and Donald. The morning unfolded in a familiar, rhythmic blur. The first hour flew by in a wave of cheerful, chattering seniors arriving for their water aerobics class. Jack greeted each one, his hands and mouth on autopilot while his mind replayed the conversation with Sarah in the car.

Around 9:30 AM, the energy in the lobby shifted. The tell-tale rumble of school buses pulling up to the main entrance signaled the start of the city's SOS program—Students On the Swim. A moment later, the doors slid open and a long, chattering line of second graders, marshaled by their teachers, began to file in. They were from one of the city's economically disadvantaged schools, here to receive their free, weekly swim lessons, a vital community service that Fairlawn was proud to host. Jack smiled, directing the lead teacher toward the aquatics staff waiting to greet them, the organized chaos a welcome and familiar part of the weekday routine.

By 10:30 AM, the SOS group was in the pool and the initial rush had subsided. Sarah walked past the front desk on her way to the aquatics office to start her shift, catching his eye and giving him a small, encouraging nod. *We're your wall,* the look seemed to say. He felt a wave of relief seeing her. At 10:37 AM, the lobby was quiet again. The classes were in session, and the regulars were settled into their workouts. Jack stood at his station, the steady rhythm of his job a fragile shield against the turmoil in his mind. He just had to get through the day. He just had to make it to 5:30, when he could meet his sister and they could figure out a plan. It was just another day at work.

### Part 3: First Response

The time was 10:44 AM. Jack came back to the front desk after using the restroom. He sat down at his station as his immediate supervisor, Donald, got up. Jack took his spot at the scan station, his heart rate still elevated from the long walk from the back of the building.

He suddenly felt an unfamiliar pressure in his chest. It wasn't a sharp pain, just a persistent, heavy twinge that felt deeply wrong. He sat there for a moment, trying to breathe through it, thinking it would go away. *It's nothing,* he told himself. *It'll pass.*

But it didn't pass. The alarming sensation remained, a constant weight that made it hard to focus. He thought back to the radio protocol briefing he'd gotten at another rec center a few weekends ago: a "First Aid" call for non-urgent issues, and a "First Response" for a true emergency. This warning sign was too big to ignore.

He paused for one last moment of indecision, wondering if he should really call for help or just try to let it pass. He looked over at Mandy, who was busy assisting a customer at her register, completely oblivious to the silent crisis unfolding just a few feet away. He was on his own.

He grabbed the radio and depressed the talk button, his hand trembling slightly. “First Response to the Front Desk please. First Response to the Front Desk!”

He saw the door to the administrative office open and the building’s assistant manager stepped out, a questioning look on his face. Seeing him, Jack instinctively grabbed his chest with his free hand, his eyes wide, trying to signal without words: *It’s me. I’m the one who needs help.*

The signal was understood. Almost immediately, supervisors and other staff came flooding out of the administrative office—among them his boss, Jennifer, and her assistant Kaitlyn right behind her. At the same time, a new commotion started at the top of the massive concrete spiral ramp. The aquatics emergency cart, pushed by Scott, appeared, followed by a contingent of lifeguards, including Sarah, their footsteps a rapid, urgent patter as they descended the ramp from the second floor.

Sarah, her lead lifeguard training kicking in, was at his side in seconds. She was wearing her city-issued swimsuit and her glasses, her expression a mask of professional focus warring with deep, personal alarm. “Jack, what’s going on?” she asked, her voice steady despite the situation.

His breathing was becoming erratic, shallow and fast, but his voice was clear. “You know what?” he said to the room at large, looking at Jennifer. “Call 9-1-1.”

The team sprang into action. Sarah and Scott were immediately at his side, getting his vitals, their movements practiced and efficient.

“Sarah? Jennifer?” Jack said, his voice clear but punctuated by the effort of his breathing. “Please let my emergency contacts know what’s going on.”

“Get the wheelchair,” Jennifer called to someone out of view, “Jack? We’re going to take you to the office so you can be in a calmer environment,” Jennifer said, her voice firm, understanding his struggle with crowds. He was wheeled into the back office where both Jennifer and Sarah started making phone calls.

Scott appeared at the door. “Sarah? I’m dismissing you for the day. Your brother needs you more than we do.”

The paramedics arrived quickly. As they entered the office, the rec center staff explained the situation. The paramedics immediately put an oxygen mask over his nose and mouth, and the flow of cool air was a welcome relief against his labored breathing. They took his vitals again, their movements swift and professional. Everything was a blur to him as the decision was made to send him to the hospital.

“Sarah,” he said, his voice slightly muffled by the mask. “My lunch is in the fridge. Take the car, go get Dad, and meet me at the hospital.” He took his sister’s hand, his nervousness evident despite his clear speech.

“I can do that. Which hospital are you taking him to?” she asked, looking at the paramedics.

“Crossroads Memorial,” They told her, and she sprang to her feet. “Let me clock out, get dressed, and get going. I will see you there!” She took her brother’s hand, while his boss kept promising that no job was worth his health and that his front desk job would be there when he returned.

### Part 4: The Mobile Emergency Room

The journey from the back office to the ambulance bay was a surreal blur. The paramedics moved with a calm, focused urgency, Sarah walking alongside the stretcher, her hand a constant, reassuring presence on his arm until they reached the ambulance's rear door. Ï will meet you at the hospital,” she said, giving his hand one last squeeze. They loaded him in, the doors closed with a solid, final-sounding thud, and for the first time since this ordeal began, he was separated from his sister.

He was inside, the engine was running… but they weren’t moving.

*Why aren't we going?* Jack thought, a fresh wave of panic rising in his chest. *The hospital is the goal, right? Let's go!* He was lying there, staring at the ceiling of the ambulance, every second that passed feeling like a lifetime.

But the two paramedics inside were a flurry of controlled, efficient activity. One of them was attaching new, sticky pads to his chest, connecting them to a monitor that beeped in time with his frantic heartbeat. "Just getting another EKG to send to the hospital," the paramedic explained, his voice calm, not seeming to notice Jack's internal terror. "Lets them know exactly what they're dealing with before we even get there."

The other paramedic was gently palpating the veins on his other arm. "Alright, Jack, you're going to feel another little poke," she said. "We're starting a second IV line, just to be safe."

He felt the prick of the needle, then watched as she expertly taped the line down. He could hear the crackle of the radio, one of the paramedics giving a calm, detailed report to someone on the other end—a string of numbers and medical terms he didn't understand. They were talking about him, about the pressure in his chest, about his vital signs.

The first paramedic checked his blood pressure again. "Pain's still there? Okay, we're giving you a little more of that nitroglycerin."

He felt a strange tingling sensation under his tongue. All this activity, all this work, was happening while they were parked just outside the front door of the rec center. It felt like an eternity, a dangerous delay. But as he watched them work, a small, dawning realization began to cut through his fear. They weren't just waiting. They were *treating* him. This wasn't just a transport vehicle; it was a tiny, mobile emergency room, and the critical first steps of his care were happening right now.

Finally, after what felt like an age, one of the paramedics gave a nod to the driver. "Alright, we're good to go."

The ambulance lurched forward, the siren finally wailing to life. The ride was a dizzying blur of motion and the sensation of speed, but it felt surprisingly short. Before he knew it, the vehicle was slowing, backing into a bay. The rear doors swung open, flooding the small space with bright, harsh light and the sounds of a busy hospital. A team of nurses and doctors was already there, waiting for him, a gurney ready to receive him. The treatment that had started in the back of a rec center was about to continue.

# Book: Heart Attack

## Chapter 1: Hospital Arrival

### Part 1: Arrival at the Emergency Room

The ambulance dropped Jack off at the emergency department of Crossroads Memorial Hospital, and he was transferred to a hospital gurney. He was taken to a triage room where paramedics began explaining his condition to a nurse. It wasn’t too long before Sarah arrived, her face etched with worry. “Dad is on the way. How are you feeling?” she asked, her hand finding his.

“My breathing seems to have calmed down a little with the oxygen,” Jack said, his voice still a bit shaky. “I’m just waiting for them to start conducting a few tests.” He squeezed her hand, grateful she was there. A pang of obligation went through him. He and Delores were supposed to be on a Valentine's Day date tonight. He should probably let her know. He fumbled for his phone and sent a quick, simple text: Had to go to the hospital. At Crossroads. Chest pain. I’m okay.

A nurse then helped him move from the gurney to an oversized recliner chair in a bay separated only by a privacy curtain, which was left open as he didn't need privacy at the moment.

A few minutes later, Delores rushed into the bay, her eyes wide. She was putting on a mask of frantic concern to hide her genuine annoyance at the inconvenience. This was her last chance to prove to Jack that she was a good, caring person, and she knew it.

“Jack! Oh my God, your text… I came as soon as I could,” she said, her voice a little too breathless. “What happened? Are you okay?”

“I don’t know,” Jack replied honestly. “I started feeling chest pain at work, and now I’m here. How did you get here so fast, anyway?”

“It was the strangest coincidence,” she said, shaking her head as if in disbelief. “My mother’s office is literally right around the corner from here. I was just leaving her work when I got your text. I ran right over.”

Before Jack could answer, Sarah stepped in, her voice calm and even. “He’s stable for now. We’re waiting for the doctors to run some tests.”

Delores’s smile tightened slightly at the calm interruption, her frantic performance momentarily undercut by Sarah’s quiet authority. Delores leaned closer to Jack, lowering her voice so only he could hear. “I was just so looking forward to tonight… We had plans, remember?” she whispered. Her tone was one of disappointment, but to Jack, it felt like a guilt trip. He was in a hospital chair with an unknown heart issue, and she was talking about their canceled date. The final red flag he had been waiting for was waving right in front of him. He knew now, with absolute certainty, that Molly, Clare, and Sarah had been right all along.

A doctor came by, and Delores fell silent as he explained to Jack that they had found something concerning and wanted to run more tests.

“Listen,” Jack said to the doctor, his voice clear. “I have an appointment with my primary care doctor soon for a stress test. If you don’t find anything definitive, I’m fine leaving and following up with her. But… if you find something seriously wrong, and you feel in good medical conscience that discharging me is a bad idea, then I’ll agree to stay.”

The doctor nodded. “That’s a very reasonable approach, Jack. Let’s see what the tests show.”

Delores’s frustration was palpable. Her plans for the evening were ruined, and now Jack was setting conditions for staying. Jermaine arrived then, his expression a mixture of fear and determination. After getting a quick update from Jack, a new doctor came by.

"Jack," the doctor said, his expression serious. "The initial tests and the EKG show some significant irregularities. In our medical opinion, it would be a very bad idea to discharge you. We need to admit you to the main hospital’s cardiac unit immediately for monitoring and more tests."

Jack looked at his father, then at Sarah, and nodded. "Okay," he said to the doctor. "I'll stay."

"Stay?" Delores repeated, her voice a sharp, incredulous hiss the moment the doctor was out of earshot. "You're going to *stay* here? We had plans, Jack! Real plans!"

Delores ignored Sarah and Jermaine, her focus entirely on Jack. "This is ridiculous. You're fine. You're just being dramatic. I had a whole evening planned, a way to make things... special." She leaned closer, her voice dropping to a low, venomous whisper meant only for him. "I was even going to offer to finally fix your little virginity problem tonight. But I guess you'd rather lie here and be coddled by your sister."

A bolt of white-hot fury shot through Jermaine. His hands clenched into fists at his sides, every protective instinct screaming at him to step forward, grab this cruel, disrespectful young woman by the arm, and throw her out of the hospital himself. Sarah, standing beside him, went rigid, her face a mask of cold, silent rage. Her own protective instincts were roaring, and she took a half-step forward, ready to verbally tear Delores apart for daring to attack her brother when he was at his most vulnerable.

But they both stopped. They caught each other's eye, a silent, agonizing agreement passing between them. Jack was an adult. This was, for the moment, his relationship. As much as they hated it, as much as every fiber of their being wanted to intervene, they had to respect his autonomy to handle it himself.

Delores leaned back, a cruel smirk on her face, completely oblivious to the violent storm she had just unleashed in the other two adults in the room. She expected to see a flush of shame on Jack's face. Instead, he just looked at her with a completely blank, genuinely confused expression. The word "virgin" registered, but only in the academic context he knew.

He tilted his head slightly. "Virgin?" he repeated, his voice quiet and questioning. "Like... a Vestal Virgin? From ancient Rome? The priestesses who tended the sacred fire for the goddess Vesta?" He was genuinely trying to understand her reference. "Or are you talking about the concept in Islam, the Houris who are companions to the faithful in paradise? I read about that in my 'World Religions' class..."

Delores just stared at him, her smirk faltering, replaced by a look of utter bafflement. Her insult had clattered uselessly to the floor. "You're... you're unbelievable," she finally sputtered.

Standing by the side of the room, Sarah couldn't help it. A single, sharp giggle escaped her, which she tried to stifle behind her hand.

Delores's head snapped toward her. "What's so funny?" she demanded.

Sarah lowered her hand, her expression once again calm and unreadable. "Oh, nothing," she said, her voice cool and even. "You wouldn't get it."

"What wouldn't I get?" Delores rounded on her, her face a mask of fury.

"You wouldn't get that the insult you just tried to use didn't land because it doesn't mean to him what it means to you," Sarah said calmly. "You wouldn't get that trying to shame him is pointless because he has nothing to be ashamed of. You wouldn't get that this," she gestured to Jack, the IV stand, the heart monitor, "is a medical emergency, not an inconvenience. But mostly," she took a small step forward, her voice dropping to a low, final, and utterly dismissive tone, "you just wouldn't get *him*. At all. And you never will."

She held Delores's furious gaze for another beat, then turned her back, pointedly returning her attention to her brother, signaling that as far as she was concerned, Delores and the conversation were completely, irrevocably over.

Across the room, Jermaine watched the entire, ugly exchange, a cold fury settling in his gut. His hand was clenched into a fist at his side. He had half a mind to step in and throw this disrespectful, cruel young woman out of the room himself. But he stopped, his gaze falling on his son. Jack was an adult. This was his relationship, his decision to make. As much as every protective instinct screamed at him to intervene, he knew he had to respect Jack's agency.

Delores was lucky, in that moment, that the family did not dismiss her. She was lucky that Jermaine chose to honor his son’s autonomy over his own powerful impulse to protect him.

### Part 2: Transfer to Main Hospital

The transport team arrived with a gurney, and the process of moving Jack from the oversized recliner began. As they prepared to wheel him out, Jack’s eyes found Sarah’s, a silent plea for stability in a world that was spinning out of control. He reached out his hand, not towards Delores, but directly and only to his sister.

Sarah took his hand without a second’s hesitation, her grip firm and reassuring. “I’m right here, Jack,” she said softly. “We’re not going anywhere.”

The journey from the ER to the main hospital was a long, surreal trek through a maze of sterile hallways. Jack lay on the gurney, staring up at the ceiling tiles flicking past, the only constant being the steady, warm pressure of Sarah’s hand in his. She walked beside the gurney the entire way, her presence a silent, unwavering anchor.

Jermaine walked on Jack's other side, his face a mask of worried resolve. Delores trailed a few steps behind them, her face a frozen picture of polite concern. Internally, however, she was seething.

*He’s holding her hand?* she thought, her eyes fixed on their linked fingers. *His sister’s hand? Not mine? What is this? She’s treating him like a child, and he’s letting her.* The intimacy of the gesture, the clear preference he showed for his sister’s comfort over hers in this moment of crisis, felt like a profound and personal insult. But she knew she was being watched by Jermaine, by the nurses. She kept her opinions locked behind a tight, false smile.

As they continued, a new, uglier thought began to worm its way into Delores's mind, a suspicion born from her own toxic worldview and her experience with Leo's "precious sisters." *This is… weird. The way they touch, the way he looks at her. It’s not normal. Is there more to this? Are they… you know?* The thought was a disgusting, jealous whisper, a complete misreading of their innocent, platonic bond, but in Delores's mind, it was a plausible explanation for her exclusion. It was a seed of suspicion that, for her, explained everything.

Through fluorescent-lit corridors, past bustling nurses' stations, and into a large, echoing elevator, Jack never let go of Sarah’s hand. The simple, physical connection was the only thing keeping his rising panic at bay.

### Part 3: Rooming It at the Hospital

Jack was moved by stretcher from the emergency room to the main hospital where a bed was waiting for him. The plan was to do a few more tests to see how the hospital wanted to tackle the cause of this. Delores set herself up on the hospital sofa while Sarah stood at Jack’s side, her mission to comfort her frightened brother. Meanwhile, Delores sighed and checked her phone, functioning as if being in the hospital was an inconvenience to her plans for the day.

“You can always go home if you want,” Jack said, noticing her impatience. “My father and my sister can take care of me while I’m in the hospital, since you don’t seem to be happy here.”

Delores gave Jack a snooty look, about to retort, when Jermaine spoke, his voice low and firm. “Young lady, if that is how you’re going to treat my son, then I’m not sure if I can give you my blessing.”

Sarah added, her own voice quiet but solid, “There’s no reason to be rude to my brother like that. I’m in full agreement with my father and you won’t have my blessing either.”

Delores froze, the united front of Jack's family catching her completely off guard. She saw the look on Jermaine’s face and the unwavering resolve in Sarah’s. She was on thin ice. She realized she was about to make a huge mistake and quickly masked her true feelings, despite still seething that Jack had wanted to hold Sarah’s hand during the transfer. She forced a look of contrition. “You’re right,” she said, her voice suddenly soft. “I’m so sorry. I’m just… worried and stressed, and it’s coming out all wrong. I’m sorry, Jack.”

Jermaine just gave a curt nod, unconvinced. The conversation shifted as a nurse came in to explain the next step. "The doctor has ordered an ultrasound of your heart to get a better look."

“Ultrasound? The thing they use to look at babies?” Jack asked, his curiosity momentarily overriding his fear.

“Yes, it can also be used for looking at other things such as your heart and blood vessels,” Jermaine explained.

Jack’s eyebrows raised. “Wow. I honestly thought they were just for pregnant women. You learn something new every day, I guess.”

Delores just nodded, feigning interest. Internally, she was ready to explode. An ultrasound? Like for a baby? Of course. He holds his sister’s hand like a toddler, they talk to him like a child, and now they’re giving him a baby test. This is ridiculous. She kept the thought locked behind a polite, neutral expression, knowing she was being watched.

A few moments later, a stretcher was brought into the room. Jack was moved onto it, still on hospital-ordered bed rest. Jermaine, Delores, and Sarah followed as a transporter wheeled him down the hall until they got to the double doors of the nuclear medicine suite. The doctor asked them to wait outside.

The world became a series of unsettling moments. The doors swished open, and he was wheeled into a room that was dark and cold. Strange, large machines with blinking lights loomed in the corners. A person in scrubs moved him from the gurney to a narrow, hard table. They pulled his gown aside and squeezed a cold, thick goo onto his chest that made him flinch. The person then took a wand-like thing and began pressing it firmly against his skin, moving it around. On a monitor he could see out of the corner of his eye, blurry black-and-white shapes swirled like storm clouds or TV static. He had no idea what he was looking at. The person in scrubs would occasionally say, “Okay, hold your breath,” or “Now, turn onto your left side a little.” The silence was punctuated by the rhythmic beeping of a machine somewhere behind him. It felt like it took forever, and yet no time at all. Finally, the goo was wiped off his chest, and he was moved back to the gurney, feeling dazed and vulnerable. He was brought back to his room while the hospital made plans to have an operating room available for him the next day.

### Part 4: Back in the Room

Back in the quiet of his new room on the cardiac floor, a nurse helped get Jack settled, explaining the various buttons on the bed and the call remote. "The sofa pulls out into a bed for one overnight guest," she mentioned kindly before leaving them to rest. "Just let us know if you need linens".

The words hung in the air, creating a sudden, thick tension. Delores, who had been sitting on the sofa, straightened up, a flicker of proprietary expectation in her eyes. *Perfect,* she thought. *It’s my duty as his girlfriend. He has to pick me.*

But Jack’s eyes didn’t go to her. They immediately found Sarah’s, and his expression was a mask of sheer panic. He didn’t have to say a word. His look was a clear, desperate plea: Please don’t make me be alone with her. I need you.

Sarah understood his intentions perfectly. She gave him a small, warm nod, her expression a silent promise.

Of course. I'm not going anywhere.

Delores observed the entire silent exchange, and a familiar surge of cold fury washed over her. *He didn’t even look at me,* she seethed internally. He looked right at HER. His SISTER? Over ME? What is wrong with him?!

Trapped by his own non-confrontational nature, Jack knew he had to verbalize the choice he’d already made with his eyes. He took a shaky breath and looked at his sister. “Sarah?” he asked, his voice quiet but firm. “Will you… will you stay with me tonight?”

“Yes, Jack,” Sarah said immediately, her voice a warm, unwavering promise. “Absolutely. Of course, I will.”

Delores felt the rejection like a physical blow, but she knew she was being watched by Jermaine. She couldn’t make a scene. She quickly masked her true feelings, forcing a bright, considerate smile.

"Oh, that's wonderful!" she said, her voice dripping with artificial sweetness. "Jack, you should absolutely have Sarah stay with you. You need to be comfortable. I would just be in the way. I'll head home and stop by tomorrow to see how you are."

She gave Jack a quick, impersonal wave and left before anyone could respond. The moment the door clicked shut behind her, both Jermaine and Sarah let out a palpable, collective sigh of relief. The tension in the room instantly dissipated.

Now that the immediate threat was gone, the emotional toll of the day crashed down on Jack. He let out a small, choked sob, tears welling in his eyes.

“Hey, hey, what’s wrong?” Sarah asked, rushing to his side.

“I just… I hated that,” he whimpered, the stress of the confrontation overwhelming him. “I hate that I had to choose. She looked so hurt. It feels like my heart is breaking.”

"Oh, buddy," Sarah whispered, and she was there in an instant, wrapping her arms around him carefully, mindful of the IV lines. He leaned into her embrace, the hug a perfect anchor of safety and genuine love. She held him, her own heart aching for the emotional turmoil he was in, for the pain of what felt like a breaking heart. Sarah had no way of knowing, of course, that the real problem had nothing to do with a broken heart, but with a heart that was actively, physically, breaking.

"You made the right choice, son," Jermaine said simply from his chair.

Jack nodded against his sister’s shoulder, the truth of his own feelings finally cutting through the fog of guilt. "I know. I think... I think I'm done with Delores." The events of the day—her annoyance, her focus on their canceled date, her possessive jealousy—played back in his mind. Then, the chilling thought he’d had earlier clicked into place with terrifying certainty. Her story about the controlling ex… she wasn't describing a boyfriend. She was describing herself.

“Good,” Jermaine said firmly. “She’s not right for you”.

“She should definitely be kicked to the curb,” Sarah added, her agreement absolute as she gently pulled back from the hug.

With Delores gone and the truth settling in, it was time to get settled into this room, his new home for at least tonight, with the daunting reality of surgery scheduled for tomorrow.

### Part 5: A Quiet Vigil

The emotional storm of the afternoon settled into a quiet, anxious vigil. With Delores gone, the hospital room became a small sanctuary for the Bennett family. Sarah pulled the sofa out into its bed configuration, setting up her space for the night, while Jermaine sat in a chair by his son’s side.

The rhythm of the hospital soon took over. A nurse came in periodically for routine vital checks, her movements efficient and practiced. The squeak of the blood pressure cart, the soft beep of the thermometer, the gentle pinch of the pulse oximeter on Jack’s finger—these became the new sounds of their reality. Jack submitted to it all with a weary resignation, the seriousness of his situation sinking in with each check-up.

Around dinnertime, a staff member brought in a tray for Jack. It was his first experience with hospital food: a pale piece of chicken, some steamed green beans, and a scoop of mashed potatoes, all under a clear plastic lid. He poked at it with his fork, the taste as bland and sterile as the room felt.

A little while later, Jermaine returned with two styrofoam containers, the slightly more appetizing smell of the hospital cafeteria filling the room. He handed one to Sarah and sat down with his own. They ate together in a comfortable quiet, their cafeteria food a small act of solidarity with Jack.

“How’s the food, son?” Jermaine asked gently.

“It’s… food,” Jack replied with a weak smile.

As they were finishing, Sarah’s phone rang. It was Molly. Sarah answered, putting the call on speaker so Jack and her dad could hear.

“Hey!” Molly’s voice was a welcome burst of energy. “We just got off work. Clare and I are on our way to you now. We’ve got Grandpa Sam and Uncle Devin with us, too. We should be there in about twenty minutes.”

“Okay, we’ll see you soon,” Sarah said, a wave of relief in her voice.

Jack leaned his head back against the pillow. The thought of seeing the rest of his family, of having his full support system around him, was a comfort. The room was quiet again, but now it was a quiet filled with anticipation for the arrival of the rest of their clan.

### Part 6: The Family Visit

A quiet knock on the door heralded the arrival of the rest of the Bennett clan. Jermaine stepped out into the hall to greet his father, Sam, and his brother, Devin, while Molly and Clare slipped into the room to see Jack. Hospital policy only allowed two visitors at a time, and the family had a quiet, unspoken agreement: to keep Jack comfortable, one of them would always be Sarah, Molly, or Clare.

“Hey, you,” Molly whispered, her voice full of affection as she and Clare flanked his bed. “How are you really feeling?”

“Tired,” Jack admitted, managing a weak smile. “And a little scared. But better now that you guys are here.”

They chatted for a few minutes in hushed tones, their presence a comforting balm. Eventually, they switched out with their father and grandfather, with Sarah remaining as the constant.

Sam Bennett, the family patriarch, looked at his grandson, his expression a mixture of love and deep concern. “Your dad told us what happened with that… young lady,” he said, his voice a low rumble.

“She’s nothing but trouble, son,” Devin added, his arms crossed. “The way Jermaine described her behavior here? Unacceptable.”

Jermaine, who had returned to the room, nodded grimly. “We all agree, Jack. She needs to be kicked to the curb.”

Jack listened to the unified, protective front of his father, uncle, and grandfather. Their certainty bolstered his own. “You’re right,” he said, his voice quiet but firm. “You’re all right. I’m starting to agree. I’m done with her.”

With that difficult chapter closed, the conversation shifted to the more immediate and frightening topic.

“The doctor came by while you were all on your way,” Sarah explained, her voice steady. “Surgery is scheduled for tomorrow morning.”

She took a deep breath. “They’re going to try a procedure called a balloon angioplasty. They’ll go in and try to inflate a little balloon to open the blood vessel.”

“That sounds… simple enough,” Sam said, though his expression was still worried.

“That’s the problem,” Jermaine interjected grimly. “The doctor said there’s a good chance it won’t work. The blockage is 99 percent, and it’s located right at the entrance to his heart, which makes it very difficult and dangerous to get to. They’ll try the angioplasty first, but we need to be prepared for it to fail.”

A heavy silence fell over the room, the true gravity of Jack’s situation settled upon them. The stakes were incredibly high. Sarah instinctively reached out and took her brother’s hand again, her grip a silent promise of support in the face of the terrifying day that lay ahead.

### Part 7: The Night Before Surgery

As visiting hours began to wind down, the rest of the family said their loving but worried goodbyes. Grandpa Sam, Uncle Devin, Molly, and Clare each took a moment to squeeze Jack’s hand, offering words of love and encouragement before leaving the room to Jack, Sarah, and Jermaine.

The quiet that settled afterward was heavy with anticipation for the morning. Jermaine turned to his daughter. “Sarah, do you need anything for tonight? I can run home and grab whatever you need for your stay.”

“Actually, yeah, that would be great, Dad,” Sarah said, grateful. “I’ll need my pajamas and a change of clothes for tomorrow. I’ve already got my laptop, so I can keep myself busy and get some homework done.”

“I’ll grab your laptop too, Jack,” Jermaine offered. “Might help you pass the time before the surgery.”

About an hour later, Jermaine returned. He carried a bag with fresh clothes for Sarah and Jack’s laptop. He also brought pillows and a thick, familiar blanket from home for Sarah’s sofa bed. The hospital provided linens, of course, but these were comforts from their own house, a small way to make the sterile room feel more like a sanctuary.

As they were arranging the items, Jack shifted uncomfortably in the bed. “I, uh… I need to use the toilet,” he said, a note of frustration in his voice. He looked down at the IV line in his arm and the monitor wires attached to his chest. He pressed the call button for the nurse. “I’m going to need some help with all this stuff.”

Jermaine and Sarah exchanged a look of understanding. While either of them would have gladly helped, they knew this was a job best left to a professional who knew how to navigate the medical equipment safely. The nurse arrived promptly and, with practiced efficiency, helped Jack get untangled and to the restroom.

Once he was helped back into the hospital bed, the vulnerability of his situation seemed to hit him again. He looked over at Sarah, who was sitting by his side. “Sarah?” he asked quietly. He held out his hand.

She took it instantly, her grip warm and sure. “I’m right here.”

They sat in silence for a moment, and then they both said the quiet thing out loud. “Next time we see her,” Sarah began, and Jack finished, “we both tell her I’m done with her.”

Jermaine, who had been watching them, nodded firmly. “That’s for the best, kids. One hundred percent.”

With that final piece of business settled, Jermaine knew it was time to go. He leaned down and hugged his son tightly. “I love you, Jack. Be strong. I’ll be here first thing in the morning.” He kissed Sarah’s forehead. “Get some rest, sweetie.” He then took his leave for the night.

Sarah dimmed the main lights in the room, leaving only a soft glow from the lamp by her sofa-bed. She settled in for the night, her laptop open but untouched, her primary focus on the quiet, steady rhythm of her brother’s breathing as they waited for the dawn.

### Part 8: The Wait for Dawn

By 9:00 PM, a heavy exhaustion had settled over the hospital room. Jack, feeling the weight of the day and the dread of the morning to come, knew he needed to at least try to sleep.

“Goodnight, Sarah,” he whispered.

“Goodnight, Jack,” she replied softly from the sofa-bed. He motioned for her to come over, and she did, leaning down to give him a hug that was a little tighter, a little longer than usual. He clung to her for a moment, drawing strength from her presence before letting go. She settled back onto the sofa with the pillows and blankets from home, the familiar comforts a small rebellion against the sterile environment.

Jack closed his eyes, trying to quiet his racing mind. But sleep wouldn't come.

Just as a fragile quiet had taken hold, at exactly 9:12 PM, the door swung open and the main fluorescent lights snapped on, flooding the room with a harsh, sterile brightness. A cheerful night nurse bustled in. “Time for one last check of your vitals!” she announced.

Jack and Sarah both groaned, blinking against the sudden glare. But they understood. His health was more important than their sleep.

As the nurse wrapped the cuff around his arm, Jack stared at the ceiling, his mind fixated on the morning. He was nervous, a deep, coiling fear in his gut. He hoped the surgery would be a success. He understood the stakes perfectly: if the balloon angioplasty worked, the doctors said he could be back on his feet, maybe even back to work at the rec center, in just a couple of days. A quick recovery. But if it failed—and they had made it clear that was a real possibility—he was facing a long, arduous road to recovery, with more, bigger surgeries to come.

The nurse finished, dimmed the lights, and left them in the quiet darkness once more.

“Try to get some sleep,” Sarah murmured from the sofa.

“You too,” Jack whispered back. But he knew he wouldn’t. He lay awake in the dim light, holding onto the memory of his sister’s hug, staring into the dark and waiting for the dawn that would decide his future.

## Chapter 2: The Day of Surgery

### Part 1: The Last Meal

The first light of February 15th, 2007, filtered into the hospital room, grey and muted. Jack was already awake when a nurse brought in his breakfast tray. Sarah stirred on the sofa bed, awakened by the quiet sounds. In the chaos and terror of the past 24 hours—her brother having a heart attack, the tense confrontation with Delores, the sleepless night—she had completely forgotten what day it was.

“Good morning,” she murmured, sitting up. She looked at the tray placed in front of her brother: a small bowl of watery oatmeal, a piece of dry toast, and a carton of apple juice.

“Morning,” Jack replied, his voice subdued.

“Right,” Sarah said, stretching. “If you get that feast, I’m heading to the cafeteria. Be right back.”

She returned a few minutes later with a breakfast sandwich wrapped in foil and a steaming cup of coffee. They ate together in a comfortable, anxious silence.

“Nervous?” Sarah asked gently, breaking the quiet.

“Terrified,” Jack admitted, pushing his toast around the plate. “I just want this to be over. And I want it to work.”

“It will,” Sarah said with a confidence she didn’t fully feel. “The doctors are the best. And I’ll be right here in the waiting room the whole time. I’ll be the first person you see when you wake up.”

He gave her a small, grateful smile. He took another bite of oatmeal, his expression hardening with resolve. “After this is over,” he said, his voice firm, “we need to deal with Delores.”

Sarah nodded, her own expression serious. “Absolutely. We deal with her together.”

“When she shows up again,” Jack continued, laying out the plan they had discussed, “we’ll both be there. No more me being alone with her. We’ll tell her it’s over, and that it’s not up for discussion. That she needs to leave me alone, for good.”

“It’s the right thing to do,” Sarah agreed. “She’s not safe for you, Jack. We all see it.”

A nurse came in then. “Alright, Jack, that’s it for food and drink until after your procedure.”

He took one last gulp of apple juice. The finality of the moment settled over them. As the nurse cleared the tray, Jack looked at his sister, his expression softening despite his own fear.

“Hey, Sarah,” he said quietly.

“Yeah?”

“Happy Birthday.”

The words hit her with the force of a physical blow. Her birthday. It was her 23rd birthday. She had completely, utterly forgotten. Her eyes instantly filled with tears, not just from the sentiment, but from the incredible, selfless love of her brother, who, while facing a terrifying and life-threatening surgery, had remembered *her*.

“Oh, Jack,” she whispered, her voice thick with emotion as she took his hand. “Don’t you worry about that right now. That doesn’t matter at all.”

“I’m sorry it’s… this,” he started to say.

“No,” she cut him off gently but fiercely. “You listen to me. The only birthday present I want this year is for you to get through this and get better. That's it. You being okay is my gift. So you just focus on that, okay?”

He squeezed her hand, a silent thank you passing between them. The plan for Delores was set. Now, all that was left was to face the surgery.

### Part 2: The Journey to the OR

The quiet of the morning was broken by a cheerful but firm knock. A hospital transporter entered the room with a gurney, the squeak of its wheels sharp in the tense silence. "Morning, Jack. Time to get you ready to go."

The moment had arrived. As the transporter and a nurse began the process of preparing him for the move, Jack glanced around the room. He was surprised Delores hadn't shown up, but he caught Sarah's eye and saw his own feeling reflected there: they were both not complaining.

As the transporter began to wheel him out, Jack reached out his hand, not towards the door, but directly to his sister. Sarah took it without a second’s hesitation, her grip firm and reassuring, just as she had during the long trek from the emergency room the day before.

She walked beside the gurney, matching its pace, their hands linked the entire way. The journey through the sterile hallways felt surreal, the squeak of the wheels and the distant, muted hospital sounds the only accompaniment to their silent communion. Finally, they arrived at a set of large, intimidating double doors marked "Surgical Suite." The transporter paused.

"This is as far as family can go," he said gently.

Sarah squeezed Jack's hand one last time before letting go.

"Wait," Jack said, his voice a little shaky. He looked at his sister. "One last hug?"

"Always," she whispered, stepping forward. She leaned over the gurney and wrapped her arms around him, holding him tightly. It was a hug that conveyed everything they couldn't say—her fear for him, her unwavering love, and a fierce promise of protection.

As she pulled back, she kept her hands on his shoulders, her gaze steady and sure. "Dad and I will be right here in the waiting room the whole time," she promised. "We're not going anywhere. I'll be the first person you see when you wake up, okay?"

Jack nodded, unable to speak past the lump in his throat. The surgical doors swished open, and the transporter began to wheel him inside. He looked back at his sister one last time before the doors closed, leaving her alone in the quiet hallway.

The surgical doors swished open, and the transporter began to wheel him inside. He looked back at his sister one last time before the doors closed, leaving her alone in the quiet hallway.

The world inside was a sudden, stark contrast. The hallway opened into a large, cold room that smelled sharply of antiseptic. A huge, multi-faceted light loomed directly over a narrow table in the center of the room. Machines with screens and wires stood like silent sentinels on all sides, beeping softly. Everyone was dressed in blue or green scrubs, their faces hidden behind masks, leaving only their focused, professional eyes visible.

“Alright, Jack, we’re just going to move you over to the table now,” a calm voice said. Hands gently but firmly shifted him from the gurney to the hard, cold operating table. The bright light overhead was blinding.

A woman with kind eyes leaned over him. “Hi, Jack. I’m Dr. Evans, from anesthesia. I’m going to be taking care of you today, making sure you’re comfortable and asleep for the procedure.”

He felt someone else placing sticky pads on his chest and an oxygen mask over his nose and mouth. “Just breathe normally for me,” another voice instructed.

“Okay, Jack,” Dr. Evans said, her voice a calm anchor in the disorienting preparations. “You’ll feel a little pinch in your arm now.” He felt the sharp prick of the IV needle. “I’m starting the medication. You might feel a little coldness in your arm. I just need you to count backward from ten for me.”

Jack stared up at the blinding light, the faces of the masked team blurring around him. He took a breath of the cool, clean oxygen, and his last conscious thought was of his sister’s promise. *Sarah will be there when I wake up.*

“Ten… nine…” he began, his voice muffled by the mask.

The beeping of the monitors seemed to grow distant.

“Eight… sev…”

The edges of his vision softened, and the bright light dissolved into a peaceful, welcoming darkness.

### Part 3: A Failed Procedure

The first thing Jack was aware of was a low, steady beeping and the murmur of serious voices. He was floating in a groggy, grey fog, unable to open his eyes.

.”..simply wouldn't inflate," one voice said, the words sharp and clinical. "Complete blockage. We were losing him. Had to abort immediately."

"So it's a failure," another voice stated. "He'll need to be transferred to the Heart Hospital for bypass. As soon as they have a bed."

The voices stopped abruptly. Jack felt a gentle touch on his shoulder. "Jack? Can you hear me? The procedure is over."

He managed to force his heavy eyelids open. The world was a blur of ceiling tiles and masked faces. He was being wheeled somewhere. The fog was thick, but one thought cut through it, clear and urgent. "Sarah?" he rasped, his throat dry and sore.

"She's waiting for you in your room," a nurse's kind voice reassured him.

As they wheeled him back into his room, his vision started to clear. The first thing he saw was his sister's face. She was trying to smile, but her eyes were red-rimmed and full of a terror he'd never seen before. She rushed to his side as he was transferred back into his bed.

"Hey," he mumbled. "It's over?"

"Yeah, it's over," she said, her voice thick. She took his hand, her grip tight.

"Why do you look so upset?" Jack asked, a new anxiety coiling in his stomach. "Did it... not work?"

Sarah's face crumpled for a second before she regained her composure. "The doctors will be in to talk to you in a minute, Jack," she said, avoiding his eyes. "They can explain everything better." She felt it was the doctors' place, not hers, to tell him the full, horrifying story.

A moment later, a doctor entered the room, his expression somber but kind. He pulled up a chair. "Jack," he began gently, "I'm sorry to have to tell you this, but the balloon angioplasty was not successful. The blockage was too severe, and we encountered a life-threatening complication."

The doctor explained what had happened: the balloon failing to inflate, the vessel becoming 100% blocked, and the surgical team having to remove it immediately to save his life. "To be very direct," the doctor said, his gaze steady, "you came very close to death on the operating table. The team's decision to abort saved you."

Jack just stared, the words not fully registering. *Close to death.*

"Because the angioplasty failed," the doctor continued, "you're going to need a more significant surgery. We're arranging a transfer to the Heart Hospital as soon as they have a bed open for you. For now, you'll stay here with us."

The doctor left them to process the news. *Close to death.* The phrase echoed in the sudden, deafening quiet of the room. It took a moment for the reality to crash down on him. He hadn't just had a failed surgery; he had almost not woken up at all. A violent tremor ran through him. He turned his head, his eyes finding his sister's.

"Sarah," he choked out, his voice breaking. He lifted his free arm, a desperate, silent plea.

She was there in an instant, wrapping her arms around him as best she could. The doctor's words crashed over her, and a new, horrifying realization twisted in her gut, sharp and agonizing. *He almost died. He almost died on my birthday.* The thought was a silent scream in her mind. Her birthday present, the one she had asked for just this morning—for him to be okay—had almost been snatched away in the most brutal way imaginable. The day of her birth would have become the day of his death. Her own tears finally fell, hot and heavy, a torrent of grief for a tragedy that had come terrifyingly close to being real.
